%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\subsection{\hspace{0.1in} Advice for Teaching Assistants (TAs)}
\label{adviceforteachingassistants}

Advice for graduate teaching assistants (GTAs), or simply TAs: \vspace{-0.3cm}
\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -4pt
\item Prepare for the classes with ample time to spare; prepare early, and know my stuff well ... if I don't know my stuff well, my students can/will assume that I do not know what I'm doing, and they'll not pay attention to the discussion session that I am leading ... think of the poor, inept TAs that I had
\item Talk to TAs and faculty who have taught the same course that I will teach, and ask them for copies of their syllabi
\item Sit in on the class that I'm going to teach
\item Find out which faculty in the department are known for being good teachers, and ask them if I can watch them teach
\item Attend conference workshops and seminars, and my department's formal GTA programs ... such as a three-day teaching orientation before the school year begins
\item Observe other GTAs teaching
\item Know my job requirements, and expectations from the course instructor and/or fellow TAs ... For example, do I have to come to class occasionally and record grades? Do I have to grade a hundred term papers and answer students' e-mails?How much time per week am I expected to work?
\item Structure weekly or biweekly meetings where I can talk with the professor about student considerations, and how I think the class is responding to the teacher overall
\item Budget time into my schedule to complete my teaching tasks
\item New TAs often underestimate how long it takes to design and grade tests, or answer student questions
\item Develop an organization system that works for me and stick with it: use \LaTeX to typeset (lecture/study/class) notes, presentation slides, assignments (homework handouts), project descriptions, and examinations.
\item Pay attention to student epistemologies: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item Some students (such as myself) learn just enough material and practice problems repetitively to get by in their engineering degree program without having a sound understanding of the fundamentals in science (including computer science) and mathematics.
	\item That is, students may favor descriptive knowledge (also known as declarative knowledge or propositional knowledge) without having a deep understanding of the fundamentals in science (including CS) and mathematics.
	\item Hence, I need to develop ways to help students (including myself) to acquire a deep understanding of the fundamentals in science (including CS) and mathematics.
	\end{enumerate}
\item For online resources, see bullet point ``Advice on teaching'' in \S\ref{gradschinfo}
\end{enumerate}




%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\subsection{\hspace{0.1in} Notes on EDA Software Development}
\label{sssec:edasoftwaredevelopment}

Notes on EDA software development: \vspace{-0.3cm}
\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -4pt
% software environment
\item {\bf Software Environment}
\item do not spend too much time setting up the software development environment: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item if so-called ``better'' software engineering tools for build automation, configuration management, documentation generation, and automated regression testing take a long time to set up, use substitute tools that are easier to learn and use
	\item When I have free time later, I can learn how to use them and integrate them into my software development environment
	\item See \S\ref{envforswdevnvlsidesign}. {\bf Seriously, don't bother with all the fancy stuff until I have a significant code base.}
	\item {\bf Remember what Alicia Lowery said. ``Don't spend so much time automating stuff, such that you hardly have time for what you're supposed to do''}
	\end{enumerate}
\item do not spend too much time on setting up experimental environments to the detriment of spending time on innovating: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item focus on advancing state-of-the-art in evaluation methodologies to off-load ourselves to be more innovative
	\end{enumerate}
\item avoid vendor lock-in: use free and open-source software as far as possible; and choose proprietary software that uses open standards and file formats, rather than those that don't
\\ \rule{6in}{.1pt}
% computer-aided software engineering (CASE) tools
\item {\bf Computer-Aided Software Engineering (CASE) Tools}
\item configuration management tools
\item data modeling tools
\item model transformation tools
\item refactoring tools/browsers: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item \colorbox{yellow}{\bf Learn to use refactoring tools!!!}
	\item techniques that allow for more abstraction: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item Encapsulate Field (field encapsulation, or data hiding) -- force code to access the field with getter and setter methods
		\item Generalize Type (type generalization) -- create more general types to allow for more code sharing
		\item Replace type -- checking code with State/Strategy
		\item Replace conditional with polymorphism
		\end{enumerate}
	\item techniques for breaking code apart into more logical pieces: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item Extract Method, to turn part of a larger method into a new method. By breaking down code in smaller pieces, it is more easily understandable. This is also applicable to functions.
		\item Extract Class moves part of the code from an existing class into a new class.
		\end{enumerate}
	\item techniques for improving names and location of code: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item Move Method or Move Field -- move to a more appropriate Class or source file
		\item Rename Method or Rename Field - changing the name into a new one that better reveals its purpose
		\item Pull Up -- in OOP, move to a superclass
		\item Push Down -- in OOP, move to a subclass
		\item Shotgun surgery -- in OOP, when a single change affects many classes. In this case, all affected code is kept in a single class, so that the change is made only in one place.
		\end{enumerate}
	\item avoid ``cut and paste coding''
	\item \colorbox{yellow}{\bf beware of technical debt (or design debt or code debt)}: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item building up technical/design debt by not refactoring regularly would eventually force a rewrite of the software, which would consume much more time than regularly refactoring the software
		\item as technical/design debt accumulates, the software architecture tends toward a big ball of mud (no distinguishable software architecture); regular refactoring of the code can avoid this
		\item avoid these: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item spaghetti code: source code that has a complex and tangled control structure, especially one using many GOTOs, exceptions, threads, or other ``unstructured'' branching constructs
			\item ravioli code: a type of computer program structure, characterized by a number of small and (ideally) loosely-coupled software components
			\item lasagna code: a type of program structure, characterized by several well-defined and separable layers, where each layer of code accesses services in the layers below through well-defined interfaces
			\end{enumerate}
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{enumerate}
\item source code generation tools
\item Unified Modeling Language modeling tools: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item \colorbox{red}{\bf Find an appropriate UML tool to use for my software projects!!!}: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item {\tt ArgoUML}
		\item {\tt Eclipse UML 2 Tools}
		\item {\tt BoUML}
		\item {\tt Dia}
		\item {\tt Acceleo}
		\item {\tt Umbrello UML Modeller}
		\item {\tt TopCased}
		\end{enumerate}
	\item alternative modeling languages and tools: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item flowchart
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{enumerate}
\item 3 categories of CASE tools: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item tools support only specific tasks in the software process
	\item workbenches support only one or a few activities
	\item environments support (a large part of) the software process
	\item {\it workbenches and environments are generally built as collections of tools}
	\end{enumerate}
\item see \url{http://www.tigris.org/servlets/ProjectList} for a list of open source software engineering tools
\\ \rule{6in}{.1pt}
% integrated development environments (IDEs)
\item {\bf Integrated Development Environments (IDEs)}
\item use integrated development environments (IDEs) for: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item source code editing: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item {\tt Aquamacs Emacs} and {\tt GNU Emacs/XEmacs} serve as decent editors for {\tt SPICE} with syntax highlighting
		\end{enumerate}
	\item autocomplete
	\item compiler and/or interpreter: \vspace{-0.3cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item {\tt javac}
		\item {\tt GCC}
		\item {\tt Intel} provides a free non-commercial version of their {\tt C++} compiler for {\tt Linux} which gets 20\% better performance on numerically intensive code than gcc by using MMX and SSE instructions; see \url{http://software.intel.com/en-us/intel-sdp-home/} ... Old link: \url{http://developer.intel.com/software/products/compilers/clin/noncom.htm}
		\item use {\tt distcc} to speed up compilation of source code by using distributed computing over a computer network. With the right configuration, {\tt distcc} can dramatically reduce a project's compilation time. This works for {\tt C}, and {\tt C++}
		\end{enumerate}
	\item build automation tools: \vspace{-0.3cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item {\tt make}
		\item the ``makedepend'' feature in {\tt GNU Make} allows some source code dependency management, and incremental build processing
		\item {\bf Familiarize myself with the GNU build system (also known as Autotools)}
		\item {\tt CMake} is a decent substitute for {\tt make}: \vspace{-0.1cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -1pt
			\item \url{http://www.cmake.org/}
			\item Also, use {\it pkg-config} with this. See \url{http://pkg-config.freedesktop.org/wiki/}.
			\end{enumerate}
		\item {\it Waf}
		\item {\it SCons}
		\item {\it Boost.Build}
		\end{enumerate}
	\item configuration management (CM): \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item {\tt Mercurial}
		\item {\tt Git}
		\item {\tt Darcs}: distributed revision control system \colorbox{yellow}{\bf Try this with Trac}
		\item {\tt Concurrent Versions System (CVS)}
		\item {\tt Subversion (SVN)}; see \url{http://www.centerstageproject.com/wiki/index.php/svn_tutorial/contents.html}
		\item {\tt GNU arch}
		\item {\tt Bazaar}; see \url{http://bazaar-vcs.org/}
		\item {\tt Fossil}: \url{http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fossil_(software)}
		\item {\tt Codeville}: \url{http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Codeville}
		\item {\tt ArX}: \url{http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ArX_(revision_control)}
		\item {\tt SVK}: \url{http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SVK}
		\item check out ``open source software hosting facilities'': \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item see \url{http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Comparison_of_free_software_hosting_facilities}
			\item {\tt Google Code}; see \url{http://code.google.com/}			
			\item {\tt GitHub}; see \url{http://github.com/}
			\item {\tt SourceForge}; see \url{http://sourceforge.net/}
			\item {\tt GNU Savannah}; see \url{http://savannah.gnu.org/}
			\item {\tt Tigris.org}; see \url{http://www.tigris.org/}
			\end{enumerate}
		\item organize the collection of source code files into a directory tree ({\bf source tree})
		\item CM tool is used to manage the {\bf code base}
		\item carry out {\tt continuous integration} to reduce integration times; that is, by reducing the changes/differences between the repository code and the developer's copy (of the repository), the amount of work required by the developer prior to the submission of their own changes is smaller; hence, continuous integration is used to avoid ``integration hell,'' where the aforementioned difference is huge; try, ``nightly builds,'' or submit a revision/build every couple of hours (prior to lunch/dinner/sleep); {\bf Automated Continuous Integration}
		\item advantages of continuous integration: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item when unit tests fail, or a bug is discovered, developers might revert the codebase back to a bug-free state, without wasting time debugging
			\item integration problems are detected and fixed continuously -- no last minute hiatus before release dates
			\item early warning of broken/incompatible code
			\item early warning of conflicting changes
			\item immediate unit testing of all changes
			\item constant availability of a ``current'' build for testing, demo, or release purposes
			\item the immediate impact of checking in incomplete or broken code acts as an incentive to developers to learn to work more incrementally with shorter feedback cycles
			\end{enumerate}
		\item use automated regression testing and test-driven development to avoid problems with configuration management
		\item tools to facilitate continuous integration: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item see \url{http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Continuous_integration}
			\end{enumerate}
		\end{enumerate}
	\item debugging: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item {\tt GDB: The GNU Project Debugger}
		\end{enumerate}
	\item GUI design and development
	\item check the code coverage
	\item static code analysis
	\item class browsing
	\item performance analysis (or profiling)
	\end{enumerate}
\item learn to take advantage of the IDE's toolchain and API browser
\item {\it check if my text editors allow me to print (and save as PDF files) syntax highlighted:} \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item {\tt SPICE} decks
	\item {\tt Verilog} code
	\item {\tt Verilog-AMS} code
	\item {\tt SystemVerilog}\texttrademark\  code
	\item {\tt SystemC}\texttrademark\ code
	\item {\tt SystemC-AMS}\texttrademark\ code
	\item {\tt VHDL} code
	\item {\tt VHDL-AMS}	 code
%	\item \MP\ code
	\end{enumerate}
\item {\bf Try Xcode and Eclipse; they are great IDEs}
\\ \rule{6in}{.1pt}
% software analysis -- performance analysis and software test/verification coverage
\item {\bf Software Analysis}
\item analyze the EDA software: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item analyze the verification/test code coverage for the software using code coverage tools: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item EMMA (code coverage tool); see \url{http://emma.sourceforge.net/} and \url{http://www.eclemma.org/}
		\end{enumerate}
	\item CPU and memory usage
	\item load capabilities
	\item use professional tools for performing such audits. 
	\end{enumerate}
\item {\tt OpenC++}: a software tool to parse and analyze C++ source code; see \url{http://opencxx.sourceforge.net/}
\item {\tt Flex++}: a parser generator for creating language parsing programs; {\tt Flex} primarily generates C code to be compiled, as opposed to {\tt C++} libraries and code. {\tt Flex++} is used for generating C++ code and classes. The {\tt Flex++} classes and code require a {\tt C++} compiler to create lexical and pattern-matching programs. {\tt Flex} defaults to generating a parsing scanner in {\tt C} code. The {\tt Flex++} generated {\tt C++} scanner includes the header file {\tt FlexLexer.h}, which defines the interfaces of the two {\tt C++} generated classes. See \url{http://flex.sourceforge.net/}
\item {\tt Instruments}\textregistered\ is a performance analyzer and visualizer for {\tt Mac OS X}\textregistered: \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -3pt
	\item Use this tool for performance analysis of my software (EDA tools) on my {\tt Mac Book Pro}\textregistered\ and {\tt PowerBook}\textregistered
	\item Instruments of change: {\tt Instruments} includes analysis instruments in six main categories \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item {\bf User events:} Tracks the exact time of user interactions, such as mouse clicks.
		\item {\bf CPU and processes:} Monitors activity, sampling, load graphs, and threads.
		\item {\bf Memory:} Tracks garbage collection, object allocations, and leaks.
		\item {\bf File activity:} Monitors disk activity, reads and writes, and file locks.
		\item {\bf Network activity:} Measures and records network traffic.
		\item {\bf Graphics:} Interprets the inner workings of the {\tt OpenGL} driver.
		\end{enumerate}
	\item Tracks that track: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item Now you can visually compare many analysis instruments side by side over time.
		\item ThatÕs because {\tt Instruments} creates a time-based record of your entire application run, storing information such as CPU load, network and file activity, and memory allocations as separate ÒtracksÓ of data.
		\item These tracks are displayed synchronized over time, allowing you to quickly identify application events -- like what was happening with the disk just before the CPU usage spiked.
		\item {\tt Instruments} gives you a complete picture of your application, so you can better understand cause-and-effect relationships and make changes to improve performance. 
		\end{enumerate}
	\item Record, replay, repeat: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item Instruments works with new {\tt Universal Access features} in {\tt Leopard} to record a user-interactive run of your application, making it easy to replay the same behavior over and over.
		\item That way, you can create ad hoc application tests anytime you like.
		\item Record the applicationÕs behavior in {\tt Instruments}, change your code, and rerun the tests to see the effect of your changes.
		\item Run your application in a template and {\tt Instruments} visually compares the runs side by side, making it clear where your code changes affect performance or memory consumption.
		\end{enumerate} 
	\item See \url{http://www.apple.com/macosx/developertools/instruments.html}
	\item Built-in instruments can track: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item User events, such as keyboard keys pressed and mouse moves and clicks with exact time.
		\item CPU activity of processes and threads.
		\item Memory allocation and releasing of memory regions, garbage collection, and memory leaks.
		\item File reads, writes, locks.
		\item Network activity and traffic.
		\item Graphics and inner workings of {\tt OpenGL}.
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{enumerate}
\item carry out performance engineering, which process includes: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item performance optimization (and performance verification)
	\item performance analysis
	\item performance tuning
	\item performance testing, including load testing, stress testing, endurance testing (soak testing), and spike testing
	\item $[${\it repeat if necessary}$]$
	\end{enumerate}
\item \colorbox{yellow}{\bf learn to use performance analysis tools}
\\ \rule{6in}{.1pt}
% software verification, testing, validation, and debugging
\item {\bf Software Testing}
\item automate regression testing; 'nuff said: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item develop a software test automation framework (STAF) that is a cross-platform, distributed software test environment
	\end{enumerate}
\item software testing: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item functional software testing: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item black box testing: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item take an external perspective of the test object to derive test cases
			\end{enumerate}
		\item white box testing: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item also known as: \vspace{-0.2cm}
				\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
				\item clear box testing
				\item glass box testing
				\item transparent box testing
				\item translucent box testing
				\item structural testing
				\end{itemize}
			\item use an internal perspective of the system to design test cases based on internal structure
			\item includes: \vspace{-0.2cm}
				\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
				\item Control flow testing
				\item Data flow testing
				\item Branch Testing
				\end{itemize}
			\end{enumerate}
		\item grey box testing: having access to internal data structures and algorithms for purposes of designing the test cases, but testing at the user, or black-box level
		\item user acceptance testing (UAT)
		\item smoke test: a preliminary to further testing, which should reveal simple failures severe enough to reject a prospective software release. In this case, the smoke is metaphorical.
		\end{enumerate}
	\item non-functional software testing: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item performance testing
		\item stability testing
		\item usability testing: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item performance: \vspace{-0.2cm}
				\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
				\item speed
				\item memory capacity
				\end{itemize}
			\item accuracy: \vspace{-0.2cm}
				\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
				\item Does it give me the right answer?
				\item Or only a vague answer?
				\end{itemize}
			\item error tolerance of software: \vspace{-0.2cm}
				\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
				\item Can it tolerate any user errors (mistakes)?
				\item If so, what kind of mistakes are tolerated?
				\item How many mistakes can it tolerate?
				\end{itemize}
			\item (ease of) recall: \vspace{-0.2cm}
				\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
				\item After a significant period of non-use (absence of usage), how much can the user remember about ``using the software''?
				\item Can the user remember how to use it?
				\item Or, at least, most of the software functions?
				\end{itemize}
			\item emotional response: \vspace{-0.2cm}
				\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
				\item How does the person feel about the tasks completed?
				\item Is the person confident, stressed?
				\item Would the user recommend this system to a friend?
				\end{itemize}
			\end{enumerate}
		\item security testing
		\item internationalization and localization
		\end{enumerate}
	\item levels of software testing: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item unit testing
		\item module testing
		\item integration testing (check for bugs at the interfaces of the modules and caused by modular interaction)
		\item system testing
		\item system integration testing (validation by user in their computing environment/platform)
		\end{enumerate}
	\item regression testing
	\item carry out alpha and beta testing before shipping the final version of software
	\item artifacts of the software testing process (testing artifacts): \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item Test case: A test case in software engineering normally consists of a unique identifier, requirement references from a design specification, preconditions, events, a series of steps (also known as actions) to follow, input, output, expected result (postcondition), and actual result.
		\item Test script: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item a set of instructions that will be performed on the system under test to test that the system functions as expected
			\item the test script is the combination of a test case, test procedure, and test data
			\end{enumerate}
		\item Test data: Multiple sets of values or data are used to test the same functionality of a particular feature. All the test values and changeable environmental components are collected in separate files and stored as test data.
		\item Test suite: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item a collection of test cases that are intended to be used to test a software program to show that it has some specified set of behaviours
			\item contains detailed instructions or goals for each collection of test cases, and information on the system configuration to be used during testing
			\item executable test suite: test suite integrated with test harness
			\end{enumerate}
		\item Test plan: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item {\bf it documents the strategy that will be used to verify and ensure that a product or system meets its design specifications and other requirements}
			\item a test specification that lets the developers (dev) know what test cases will be used by the software quality assurance (QA) team
			\item a systematic approach to testing a system, such as a machine or software
			\item the plan typically contains a detailed understanding of what the eventual workflow will be
			\item it includes one or more of the following: \vspace{-0.2cm}
				\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
				\item Design Verification, or Compliance test: to be performed during the development or approval stages of the product, typically on a small sample of units
				\item Manufacturing/Production test: to be performed during preparation or assembly of the product in an ongoing manner for purposes of performance verification and quality control
				\item Acceptance/Commissioning test: to be performed at the time of delivery or installation of the product
				\item Service and Repair test: to be performed as required over the service life of the product
				\item Regression test: to be performed on an existing operational product, to verify that existing functionality didn't get broken when other aspects of the environment are changed (e.g., upgrading the platform on which an existing application runs)
				\end{itemize}
			\item three major elements of a test strategy, which should be described in the test plan: \vspace{-0.2cm}
				\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
				\item Test Coverage: \vspace{-0.2cm}
					\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
					\item states what requirements will be verified during each stage of the product life
					\item derive test coverage from design specifications and other requirements, such as safety standards or regulatory codes
					\item each requirement or specification of the design would have one or more corresponding means of verification
					\item test coverage for different product life stages may overlap
					\item test coverage also feeds back into the design process, since the product may have to be designed to allow test access; see Design For Testability (DFT)
					\end{itemize}
				\item Test Methods: \vspace{-0.2cm}
					\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
					\item they state how test coverage will be implemented
					\item they may be determined by standards, regulatory agencies, or contractual agreement, or created ad hoc
					\item they also specify test equipment to be used in the performance of the tests and establish pass/fail criteria
					\item test methods used to verify hardware design requirements can range from very simple steps, such as visual inspection, to elaborate test procedures that are documented separately
					\end{itemize}
				\item Test Responsibilities: \vspace{-0.2cm}
					\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
					\item include what organizations will perform the test methods, and at each stage of the product life
					\item allow test organizations to plan, acquire, or develop test equipment and other resources necessary to implement the test methods for which they are responsible
					\item indicate what data will be collected, and how that data will be stored and reported (often referred to as ``deliverables'')
					\item one outcome of a successful test plan should be a record or report of the verification of all design specifications and requirements as agreed upon by all parties
					\end{itemize}
				\end{itemize}
			\end{enumerate} 
		\item Test harness: The software, tools, samples of data input and output, and configurations
		\item Traceability matrix: A table that correlates requirements (or design documents) to test documents. It is used to change tests when the source documents are changed, or to verify that the test results are correct.
		\item Testbed: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item A platform for experimentation for large development projects.
			\item Testbeds allow for rigorous, transparent and replicable testing of scientific theories, computational tools, and other new technologies.
			\item A sandbox is a testing (or virtual) environment that isolates untested code changes and experimentation from the production environment or repository, in the context of software development including Web development and revision control, and by extension in web-based editing environments including wikis.
			\end{enumerate}
		\item Test oracle: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item a mechanism used to determine whether a test has passed or failed
			\item to determine whether the software passes the test case for input(s) of a given test case, it compares the output(s) of the system under test to the expected output(s)
			\item oracles are always separate from the system under test
			\item common oracles include: \vspace{-0.2cm}
				\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
				\item specifications and documentation
				\item a substitute software for carrying out the same function, while using different algorithm(s) and heuristic(s)
				\item 
				\end{itemize}
			\end{enumerate}
		\end{enumerate}
	\item phases in a software testing cycle: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item requirements analysis and design phase: QA team work with developers in determining what aspects of a design are testable, and the required parameters for those tests to work
		\item test planning: test strategy, test plan, and testbed creation
		\item test development: test procedures, test scenarios, test cases, test datasets, and test scripts
		\item test execution: QA team execute/run the software based on the test plan, and carry out bug reporting (and/or feature request -- missing feature?)
		\item test reporting: provide test reports on the test effort, and indicate whether the software is ready for release based on selected metrics indicated in the test plan
		\item test result analysis, or defect analysis: the development team and the users decide what defects should be treated, fixed, rejected (i.e., false positive), or deferred (with respect to making a decision)
		\item retesting the resolved defects: once a defect has been dealt with by the development team, it is retested by the testing team
		\item regression testing: it is common to have a small test program built of a subset of tests, for each integration of new, modified or fixed software, in order to ensure that the latest delivery has not ruined anything, and that the software product as a whole is still working correctly
		\item test closure: once the test meets the exit criteria, the activities such as capturing the key outputs, lessons learned, results, logs, and documents related to the project are archived and used as a reference for future projects
		\end{enumerate}
	\item Scenario testing uses scenario tests (or scenarios) that are based on a hypothetical story to help a person think through a complex problem or system for a testing environment: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item The ideal scenario has five key characteristics: it is: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item a story that is
			\item motivating
			\item credible
			\item complex, and
			\item easy to evaluate
			\end{enumerate}
		\item These tests are usually different from test cases; test cases cover a single step, whereas scenarios cover a number of steps
		\item Test suites and scenarios can be used together for complete system testing
		\end{enumerate}
	\item All-pairs testing or pairwise testing is a combinatorial software testing method that, for each pair of input parameters to a system (typically, a software algorithm), tests all possible discrete combinations of those parameters. Using carefully chosen test vectors, this can be done much faster than an exhaustive search of all combinations of all parameters, by ``parallelizing'' the tests of parameter pairs. The number of tests is typically {\it O(nm)}, where n and m are the number of possibilities for each of the two parameters with the most choices.
	\end{enumerate}
\item use {\tt Expect}, which is a Unix automation and testing tool, to facilitate automated regression testing
\item a good bug report includes a stack trace and a set of reduced test cases
\item before filing a bug report: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -4pt
	\item verify that it exists in the latest build
	\item next, verify it hasn't been filed already
	\item next, file the bug using the bug reporting template
	\end{enumerate}
\item keep refining my {\tt software quality assurance} process, along with my software development process
\item {\tt GM seed} (or GM build): GM stands for Gold Master. It's the finalized firmware before public release. 
\\ \rule{6in}{.1pt}
% Debugging, including memory debugging
\item {\bf Debugging, Including Memory Debugging}
\item do bug scrubbing whenever I find a bug: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item don't put off bug scrubbing until a later date, because I probably won't do it then and procrastinate again
	\item compound bugs/faults are harder to resolve
	\item fault masking makes debugging considerably harder
	\end{enumerate}
\item use {\tt Valgrind}, {\tt Insure++}, and {\tt IBM Rational Purify} for: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item memory debugging
	\item memory leak detection
	\item memory corruption detection (buffer overflows)
	\item software profiling (software performance analysis)
	\item e.g., use runtime analysis to detect memory corruption detection (buffer overflows) and memory leak detection
	\end{enumerate}
\item \colorbox{yellow}{\bf learn to use GDB very well for debugging}
\item \colorbox{yellow}{\bf learn to use software formal verification tools}
\item \colorbox{yellow}{\bf learn to use bug tracking systems, ECO management systems, or issue tracking systems}; also, see bug databases: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item {\tt Bugzilla}
	\item {\tt Trac}
	\item {\tt Atlassian Jira}
	\item {\tt LibreSource}
	\item {\tt SharpForge}
	\end{enumerate}
\item check out distributed bug trackers, such as ``DisTract'' and ``Bugs Everywhere''
\item {\bf improve the way I perform dynamic memory management}
\item {\bf Boehm-Demers-Weiser garbage collector}, {\bf Boehm GC}, or {\bf Boehm garbage collector}; see \url{http://www.hpl.hp.com/personal/Hans_Boehm/gc/}, \url{http://developers.sun.com/solaris/articles/libgc.html}, \url{http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boehm_garbage_collector}, or \url{http://sourceforge.net/projects/bdwgc/}
\item Unusual software bugs: \url{http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unusual_software_bug}
\item Links: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item \url{http://www.debugging-guide.com/tools.html}
	\item \url{http://www.debugging-guide.com/links.html}
	\end{enumerate}
\ \\ \rule{6in}{.1pt}
% formal technical reviews and software reviews
\item {\bf Formal Technical Reviews \& Software Reviews}
\item software review: \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item ``A process or meeting during which a software product is $[$examined by$]$ project personnel, managers, users, customers, user representatives, or other interested parties for comment or approval''
	\item software product: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item any technical document or partial document, produced as a deliverable of a software development activity
		\item may include documents such as: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item contracts
			\item project plans and budgets
			\item requirements documents
			\item specifications
			\item designs
			\item source code
			\item user documentation
			\item support and maintenance documentation
			\item test plans
			\item test specifications
			\item standards
			\item and any other type of specialist work product
			\end{enumerate}
		\end{enumerate}
	\item include: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item Software peer reviews are conducted by the author of the work product, or by one or more colleagues of the author, to evaluate the technical content and/or quality of the work
		\item Software management reviews are conducted by management representatives to evaluate the status of work done and to make decisions regarding downstream activities
		\item Software audit reviews are conducted by personnel external to the software project, to evaluate compliance with specifications, standards, contractual agreements, or other criteria
		\end{enumerate}
	\item values of software reviews: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item defect detection process: makes it less costly to find bugs than software testing or field use
		\item defect prevention process: identify and remove process inadequacies that encourage defects
		\item Early and frequent reviews of small work samples can identify systematic errors in the authors' work processes, which can be corrected before further erroneous work is done. This can help the authors improve their skills and significantly reduce the time it takes to write a high-quality technical document, and dramatically decrease the error rate in using the document in downstream processes.
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{enumerate}
\item formal technical review: \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item also known as: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item software inspection
		\item code inspection
		\item code review
		\item code walkthrough
		\item engineering peer review
		\item product peer review
		\item software peer review 
		\item technical peer review
		\end{enumerate}
	\item ``peer review of any work product by trained individuals who look for defects using a well defined process''
	\item ``designer or programmer leads members of the development team and other interested parties through a software product, and the participants ask questions and make comments about possible errors, violation of development standards, and other problems''
	\item ``a team of qualified personnel ... examines the suitability of the software product for its intended use and identifies discrepancies from specifications and standards. Technical reviews may also provide recommendations of alternatives and examination of various alternatives''
	\item ``A code review can be done as a special kind of inspection in which the team examines a sample of code and fixes any defects in it. In a code review, a defect is a block of code which does not properly implement its requirements, which does not function as the programmer intended, or which is not incorrect but could be improved (for example, it could be made more readable or its performance could be improved). In addition to helping teams find and fix bugs, code reviews are useful for both cross-training programmers on the code being reviewed and for helping junior developers learn new programming techniques.''
	\item ``The goal of the inspection is to identify defects''
	\item ``The purpose of a technical review is to arrive at a technically superior version of the work product reviewed, whether by correction of defects or by recommendation or introduction of alternative approaches. While the latter aspect may offer facilities that software inspection lacks, there may be a penalty in time lost to technical discussions or disputes which may be beyond the capacity of some participants.''
	\item roles of participants: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item Moderator / Walkthrough Leader / Review Leader: can be the team manager/leader (preferable)
		\item Inspectors / Technical Staff / Reviewer
		\item Author
		\item Reader
		\item Recorder/Scribe
		\item {\it Decision Maker:} can be the team manager/leader (preferable), or the author (bad idea); this may also be carried out by the Review Leader
		\item {\it Management Staff}
		\item {\it Customer, or User Representatives}
		\end{enumerate}
	\item steps in this process: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item entry evaluation: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item the Review Leader uses a standard checklist of entry criteria to ensure that optimum conditions exist for a successful review
			\item Is the artifact ready for review?
			\item Here, the artifact refers to: \vspace{-0.2cm}
				\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
				\item software
				\item IC design
				\item software/IC module
				\item document
				\end{itemize}
			\end{enumerate}
		\item management preparation: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item management staff manages resources (time, materials, and tools) required/needed for the review
			\item they also manages how the reviews shall be conducted according to policies, standards, or other relevant criteria
			\end{enumerate}
		\item planning $[$the review$]$: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item the moderator staff plans the review/inspection
			\item the Review Leader identifies or confirms the objectives of the review
			\item the Review Leader also organizes a team of Reviewers
			\item in addition, the Review Leader ensures that the team is equipped with all necessary resources for conducting the review
			\end{enumerate}
		\item overview meeting (or ``overview of review procedures''): \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item The Review Leader ensures that all Reviewers understand: \vspace{-0.2cm}
				\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
				\item the review goals
				\item the review procedures
				\item the materials available to them
				\item the procedures for conducting the review
				\end{itemize}
			\item the author presents the background information of the artifact, which helps the reviewers acquire the necessary knowledge to carry out the review and put things in perspective
			\end{enumerate}
		\item $[$Individual$]$ preparation: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item each Reviewer prepares for group examination of the artifact by examining it carefully for anomalies (potential/possible defects), the nature of which will vary with the type of review and its goals
			\item each inspector shall record all identified ``potential/possible defects''
			\item possible defect: Is this a defect? Can this be a defect?
			\item potential defect: \vspace{-0.2cm}
				\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
				\item Can this lead to a defect? Or, can it become a defect later on?
				\item If so, identify this as a ``potential defect,'' so that it can be discussed at the ``inspection meeting''; at the ``inspection meeting,'' the Reviewers can suggest modifications that can prevent failures/errors from occurring
				\end{itemize}
			\item defects can be major or minor
			\end{enumerate}
		\item inspection meeting (or ``group examination''): \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item during this meeting the reader reads through the artifact, part by part, and the inspectors point out the defects for every part
			\item consolidate the findings of the Reviewers (from their individual preparation)
			\item arrive at a consensus regarding the status of the artifact (or activity) being reviewed, and come up with an action plan addressing each defect; the defects in the action plan include potential defects
			\end{enumerate}
		\item rework: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item the author(s) make(s) changes to the artifact/process to correct the errors/defects or resolve the failures
			\item this is carried out according to the action plan(s) from the inspection meeting
			\item if the action plan cannot be adhered to, the author(s) should indicate why and bring that up to the Review Leader and committee of Reviewers; if necessary, another review may be held to modify the action plan (if necessary, repeat the process)
			\end{enumerate}
		\item follow-up: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item the Review Leader checks that the author has made changes according to the action plan
			\item if changes are not made according to the action plan, go back to the {\tt rework} stage/phase
			\end{enumerate}
		\item The process is terminated by the moderator when it satisfies some predefined exit criteria: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item all action plans must have been adhered to and completed
			\item the Review Leader also checks that documents used in this review been kept in the repository under a change management (or configuration management) process
			\end{enumerate}
		\end{enumerate}
	\item use a software repositories based on {\tt Subversion} with {\tt Trac}, {\tt Mercurial}, or {\tt GIT} to allow people to collaboratively review code; {\tt specific tools for collaborative code review can facilitate the code review process}: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item {\tt Trac}: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item an open source, web-based project management (roadmap \& milestones) and bug-tracking tool
			\item {\tt Trac} allows hyperlinking information between a computer bug database, revision control and wiki content
			\item {\tt Trac} also serves as a web interface to a version control system like: \vspace{-0.2cm}
				\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
				\item {\tt Mercurial}
				\item {\tt Git}
				\item {\tt Subversion}
				\item {\tt Bazaar}
				\item {\tt Darcs}: {\bf Try this with Trac}
				\end{itemize}
			\item RSS Feeds
			\item \colorbox{yellow}{\bf If I can draw Gantt charts with this, USE THIS!!!}
			\end{enumerate}
		\item {\tt Mercurial}: a cross-platform, distributed revision control tool; $\exists$ a plugin for Eclipse
		\end{enumerate}
	\item use ``automated code review software'' to facilitate the code review process
	\item categories of code review process: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item formal code review
		\item lightweight code review (includes pair programming)
		\end{enumerate}
	\item be objective
	\item do not make this a scoring process: ``Oh, I found more bugs in your code''
	\item they differ from: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item management reviews: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item conducted by management representatives, rather than by colleagues
			\item conducted for management purposes (e.g., promotion, bonuses, and incentive pay), rather than for technical evaluation
			\end{enumerate}
		\item software audit reviews: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item conducted by personnel external to the project
			\item they evaluate compliance with specifications, standards, contractual agreements, or other criteria
			\end{enumerate}
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{enumerate}
\rule{6in}{.1pt}
% code conventions, code documentation guidelines, coding guidelines, coding scheme, coding standards, coding style guide, coding styles, programming guidelines, and programming style
%	For my BibTeX database, use: coding scheme, coding standards, coding styles, coding guidelines, and programming style
\item {\bf Coding Standards, Code Documentation Guidelines, and Coding Style Guide}
\item Documentation generators that I am using: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item Doxygen (for C++, C, Java, Python, Fortran, and VHDL)
	\item DOC++ (for C/C++ and Java)
	\item ROBODoc (for Ruby)
	\item RDoc (for Ruby)
	\item Sphinx (for Python): \url{http://sphinx.pocoo.org/}
	\item Javadoc (for Java)
	\item Haddock (for Haskell)
	\item DocBook (semantic markup language) that can produce documents in various formats. {\bf Note: I am not sure if it can parse source code, and churn out the appropriate documentation (e.g., API) in PDF, as man pages, and in HTML. While I can use it for documentation of my projects, I may as well use \LaTeX\ since I would have to use \LaTeX\ for my research publications anyway.}
	\end{enumerate}
\item My coding style should follow that of {\tt Javadoc}, which is similar to that used by Doxygen. My indent style would be the {\tt 1TBS} variant of the {\tt K\&R} style, which is an abbreviation of ``{\tt The One True Brace Style}''. It is also equivalent to the {\tt Kernel Normal Form style} (or {\tt BSD KNF style}).
\item Tags that I would use in my comments are: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item @author {\it Author's\_Name}: indicate the author ({\it Author's\_Name}) of the file/function
	\item @version {\it X.Y}: indicate the version ({\it X.Y}) of the file
	\item @section {\it SECTION\_NAME}: indicate the section ({\it SECTION\_NAME}) of the file, which can be: {\it LICENSE} or {\it DESCRIPTION}
	\item @param {\it x}: indicate the parameter ({\it x}) of the constructor or function
	\item @exception {\it Exception\_Name}, or @throws {\it Exception\_Name}: an exception that a function/method can throw
	\item @return {\it Return\_Statement}: indicate the return (type and) action of the function
	\item @see {\it reference}: a link to another element in the documentation; e.g., @see {\it Class\_Name}, or @see {\it Class\_Name\#member\_function\_name}
	\item @since {\it X.Y: Month-Day-Year}: This functionality has been added since version {\it X.Y} (and on the date {\it Month-Day-Year})
	\item @deprecated {\it description}: Describe an outdated function/method, and indicate when the function/method has deprecated
	\item ``@link {\it ... URL...} @endlink'' is used to include hyperlinks in the generated documentation for Doxygen
	\item \#\#\#\# IMPORTANT NOTES: Notes that are critical for helping the reader understanding assumptions and decisions made while developing the software
	\item @todo($<$message$>$, $<$version$>$) (or \#\#\#\# TO BE COMPLETED): Task to be finished at a later time
	\item \#\#\#\# TO BE FIXED: Task to be debugged at a later time
	\item @migration($<$message$>$, $<$version$>$): Code is being migrated to another function/method, or class.
	\item See \url{http://www.stack.nl/~dimitri/doxygen/commands.html} for more information of tags that are recognized by {\tt Doxygen}.
	\item @pre (or @precondition): Precondition(s) of the function.
	\item @assert: Assertion(s) of the function.
	\item @post (or @postcondition): Postcondition(s) of the function.
	\end{enumerate}
\item The order of tags in different sections of the code is given as follows: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item Headers/Interfaces and Classes: @version, @author, and @see
	\item Constructors: @param, @exception, and @see
	\item Functions/Methods: @param, @return, @exception, and @see
	\item Variables can use the @see tags.
	\item The @deprecated tag can be used for headers/interfaces, classes, constructors, functions/methods, and variables.
	\end{enumerate}
\item Since I am using Doxygen for generating documentation, I can use \LaTeX\ to provide richer markup.
\item Use {\tt ROBODoc} and {\tt RDoc} for {\tt Ruby}, and the latter for {\tt Tcl} and {\tt Perl}. The latter also generates documentation in \LaTeX, {\tt HTML}, and {\tt UNIX man pages}.
\item For a suggested coding style for {\tt Python} scripts, see \url{http://www.python.org/dev/peps/pep-0008/}.
\item For a suggested coding style for {\tt Ruby} scripts, see \url{http://www.caliban.org/ruby/rubyguide.shtml}.
\item Document the known bugs for each function/method.
\item {\tt GNU coding standards}; see \url{http://www.gnu.org/prep/standards.html} and \url{http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/GNU_Coding_Standards}; also see \url{http://www.ronancrowley.com/Eclipse_GNU_Style.xml} for ``{\tt Eclipse} Code Style Formatter for {\tt GNU Coding Standards}''
\item FMG Programming Guidelines (FPG); see \url{http://www-ti.informatik.uni-tuebingen.de/~fmg/guidelines.html} ... Also, see \url{http://www.boost.org/development/requirements.html} and \url{http://cm.bell-labs.com/cm/cs/tpop/}
\item {\tt Gnits Standards}: a collection of standards and recommendations for programming, maintaining, and distributing software; see \url{http://www.gnu.org/software/womb/gnits/} 
\item {\tt Mozilla} Coding Style Guide; see \url{https://developer.mozilla.org/en/Mozilla_Coding_Style_Guide} or \url{http://www.mozilla.org/hacking/mozilla-style-guide.html}
\item {\tt Linux} Kernel Coding Style (or Documentation/CodingStyle in the {\tt Linux} Kernel source tree); see \url{http://lxr.linux.no/source/Documentation/CodingStyle}
\item code documentation guidelines; \\see \url{http://www-ti.informatik.uni-tuebingen.de/~fmg/guidelines.html}
\item The {\tt NetBSD} source code style guide; see \url{ftp://ftp.netbsd.org/pub/NetBSD/NetBSD-current/src/share/misc/style}
\item use a good {\bf documentation generator}, such as \texttt{Doxygen} or \texttt{Javadoc}, to generate API documentation, end-user guide (or user manual), and call graphs from commented source code files; special tags (or metadata/annotations) within source code comments are often used to process documentation
\item terms used interchangeably to describe this: \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item coding standards
	\item code documentation guidelines
	\item coding style guide
	\item programming style
	\item code conventions
	\end{enumerate}
\item this covers: \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item the layout of the source code; code appearance; the visual appearance of source code; code formatting
	\item indent style
	\item the use of white space around operators and keywords
	\item naming conventions: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item the style and spelling of user-defined identifiers, such as function, procedure, and variable names
		\item the capitalization or otherwise of keywords and variable names
		\item common elements: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item length of identifiers
			\item letter case and numerals
			\item multiple-word identifiers: \vspace{-0.2cm}
				\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
				\item delimiter-separated words
				\item letter-case separated words
				\end{itemize}			
			\end{enumerate}
		\item examples of naming conventions include: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item CamelCase / ``camel case'' / medial capitals
			\item Hungarian notation
			\end{enumerate}
		\end{enumerate}
	\item the use and style of comments: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item block comments (or prologue comments)
		\item line comments (or inline comments)
		\item code descriptions: comments can be used to summarize code or to explain the programmer's intent
		\item algorithmic description: comments may contain an explanation of the methodology; such explanations may include diagrams and formal mathematical proofs
		\item resource inclusion: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item logos, diagrams, and flowcharts consisting of ASCII art constructions can be inserted into source code formatted as a comment
			\item copyright notices can be embedded within source code as comments
			\item Binary data may also be encoded in comments through a process known as binary-to-text encoding, although such practice is uncommon and typically relegated to external resource files
			\end{enumerate}
		\item debugging: a common developer practice is to comment out a code snippet, so that it will not be executed in the final program
		\end{enumerate}
	\item the use or avoidance of particular programming constructs (such as GOTO statements)
	\item left-hand comparison style: place constants or expressions to the left in any comparison
	\item declarations
	\end{enumerate}
\item see \url{http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Programming_style} for examples of programming styles: \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item \url{http://google-styleguide.googlecode.com/svn/trunk/cppguide.xml}
	\item \url{http://en.wikibooks.org/wiki/C%2B%2B_Programming/Code_Style}
	\item \url{http://www.possibility.com/Cpp/CppCodingStandard.html}
	\item \url{http://www.state-machine.com/doc/AN_QL_Coding_Standard.pdf}
	\item \url{http://geosoft.no/development/cppstyle.html}
	\item \url{http://www.qhull.org/road/road-faq/xml/cpp-guideline.xml}
	\item \url{http://java.sun.com/docs/codeconv/}
	\item \url{http://www.ambysoft.com/essays/javaCodingStandards.html}
	\item \url{http://geosoft.no/development/javastyle.html}
	\item \url{http://perldoc.perl.org/perlstyle.html}
	\item \url{http://rpa-base.rubyforge.org/wiki/wiki.cgi?GoodAPIDesign}
	\item \url{http://www.caliban.org/ruby/rubyguide.shtml}
	\item \url{http://www.python.org/peps/pep-0008.html}
	\item \url{http://httpd.apache.org/dev/styleguide.html}
	\item \url{http://drupal.org/coding-standards}
	\item \url{http://pear.php.net/manual/en/standards.php}
	\item \url{http://www.chris-lott.org/resources/cstyle/}
	\item TIOBE Software BV, {\it TIOBE Coding Standard Methodology}: \url{http://www.tiobe.com/index.php/content/paperinfo/HowToUseCodingStandards.html}
	\item Virginia Tech: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item Department of Computer Science: \vspace{-0.1cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -1pt
			\item Clifford A. Shaffer, ``Elements of Programming Style: Department of Computer Science, Computer Program Documentation Standards: Version 1.3,'' for the {\it Data Structures and File Processing} class, Department of Computer Science, Virginia Tech (Virginia Polytechnic Institute and State University). Available online at: \url{http://ei.cs.vt.edu/~cs2604/Standards/Standards.html}; last accessed on September 30, 2010.
			\end{enumerate}
		\end{enumerate}
	\item books: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item B.ÊW. Kernighan and P.ÊJ. Plauger. The Elements of Programming Style. McGraw-Hill, New York, NY, second edition, 1982. [ Also, see the following summary. Tae Yano, ``Quote from `The Elements of Programming Style' by Kernighan and Plauger,'' Computer Science Department, School of Computer Science, Carnegie Mellon University. Available online at: \url{http://www.cs.cmu.edu/~taey/pub/element_of_p_style.txt}; last accessed on September 30, 2010. ] \cite{Kernighan1982}
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{enumerate}
\item certain tags are used in comments to assist in indexing common issues: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item examples of tag conventions include: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item FIXME to mark potential problematic code that requires special attention and/or review
		\item NOTE to document inner workings of code and indicate potential pitfalls
		\item TODO to indicate planned enhancements
		\item XXX to warn other programmers of problematic or misguiding code
		\end{enumerate}
	\item There is a risk that tags accumulate over time; it is advisable to include the date and the tag owner in the tag comment to ease tracking
	\end{enumerate}
\item source code is not always self-explanatory
\item comments that are superfluous, excessive, or difficult to maintain MUST be omitted
\item the level of detail and description of comments may vary considerably; {\bf good comments clarify intent}
\item do not include offensive comments in the source code
\item place the comments in-context, so that the comment is relevant to the code adjacent to it
\\ \rule{6in}{.1pt}
% hybrid software involving multiple computer languages, such as a programming language (C++) and a scripting language (Python)
\item {\bf Hybrid Software Involving Multiple Computer Languages}
\item use the {\tt Swig} software tool and/or the {\tt Boost Python} {\tt C++} library to integrate {\tt C++} and {\tt Python} code, and to provide seamless operability between them
\item {\tt C++/Tcl} is a software programming library interface which allows the integration of {\tt C++} into {\tt Tcl} and vice versa: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item the {\tt C++/Tcl} library offers the following features: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item Support for both extending {\tt Tcl} with {\tt C++} modules and embedding {\tt Tcl} in {\tt C++} applications
		\item Possibility to expose free {\tt C++} functions as commands in {\tt Tcl}
		\item Possibility to define classes and class member functions, visible in {\tt Tcl} in the style similar to {\tt SWIG} wrappers
		\item Possibility to manipulate {\tt Tcl} lists and objects from the {\tt C++} code
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{enumerate}
\item {\tt Tcl/Java} is a project to bridge {\tt Tcl} and {\tt Java}: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item The {\tt Tcl/Java} project's goal is to make integrating the {\tt Java} platform and the {\tt Tcl} scripting language as easy as possible
	\item {\tt Tcl/Java} consists of two packages, {\tt Jacl} and {\tt Tcl Blend}
	\item {\tt Jacl}: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item a {\tt Tcl} interpreter that is written in {\tt Java}
		\item a self-contained implementation of a {\tt Tcl} interpreter, written entirely in {\tt Java}
		\item run {\tt Tcl} within the {\tt JVM}
		\item {\tt Jacl} also includes features that facilitate communication between a {\tt Java} interpreter and a {\tt Tcl} interpreter
		\item {\tt Jacl} is typically used to incorporate scripting functionality into an existing {\tt Java} application
		\item doesn't require the user to deal with the complexities of native code that come with {\tt Tcl Blend}
		\item {\bf Jacl} is used to integrate {\bf Tcl} scripts into {\bf Java} applications
		\end{enumerate}
	\item {\tt Tclblend}: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item call {\tt Java} class libraries from the {\tt C} runtime using {\tt JNI} and an embedded {\tt JVM}
		\item {\tt Tcl Blend} is a {\tt Tcl} extension that makes use of {\tt JNI} to facilitate communication between a {\tt Java} interpreter and a {\tt Tcl} interpreter
		\item {\tt Tcl Blend} is typically used to load a {\tt Java} interpreter into an existing {\tt Tcl} process, so that functionality implemented in {\tt Java} can be accessed via {\tt Tcl}
		\item One can also load {\tt Tcl Blend} and {\tt Tcl} into a {\tt Java} process, which is a great way to add scripting functionality to an existing {\tt Java} application
		\item Because {\tt Tcl Blend} is a normal {\tt Tcl} extension, one can use it with other popular {\tt Tcl} extensions like {\tt Tk}, {\tt Expect}, and {\tt Itcl}
		\item {\bf {\bf Tcl Blend} is used to call/invoke {\bf Java} methods on {\bf Java} objects that are allocated in {\bf Tcl} scripts}
		\end{enumerate}
	\item {\tt Tcl Blend} and {\tt Jacl} define both a {\tt Tcl} API and a {\tt Java} API that make it easy to call {\tt Java} code from {\tt Tcl} or call {\tt Tcl} code from {\tt Java}
	\item For example, one could allocate a {\tt Java} object in a {\tt Tcl} script and interactively invoke {\tt Java} methods on the object
	\item It is also easy to use the supplied API to evaluate a {\tt Tcl} procedure from a {\tt Java} method or implement {\tt Tcl} procudures in {\tt Java}
	\item The flexible API and wealth of implementation options provided by the {\tt Tcl/Java} project make integrating {\tt Tcl} and {\tt Java} easy
	\end{enumerate}
\item also, see {\tt Jython} and {\tt JRuby}
\\ \rule{6in}{.1pt}
% concurrent programming and scalability
\item {\bf Concurrent Programming and Scalability}
\item Does the EDA application scale (well)?: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item Does it scale for larger input netlists (or system-level or behavioral RTL design models)?
	\end{enumerate}
\item Also, does it scale well for parallel computers with more processor nodes/cores? ... Scale my EDA tool for: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item multicore processors
	\item many-core processors
	\item 64-bit processors
	\item GPGPU computing platform / GPU accelerators
	\item FPGA implementation
	\item Linux cluster
	\item server farm???
	\item grid computing
	\item distributed computing
	\item hardware accelerator / accelerator boards
	\end{enumerate}
\item learn about different APIs for concurrent programming, such as: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item {\tt POSIX Threads}; see \url{http://developer.apple.com/mac/library/samplecode/PThreadSorts/index.html} for an example of using POSIX threads (pthreads) to sort pictures.
	\item {\tt OpenMP}
	\item {\tt MPI}
	\item {\tt UPC}
	\item {\tt Intel Threading Building Blocks}
	\item {\tt Boost.Thread}
	\item {\tt OpenCL} (Open Computing Language) framework: for {\bf stream processing}; see \url{http://www.khronos.org/registry/cl/}, \url{http://www.khronos.org/opencl/sdk/1.0/docs/man/xhtml/}, and \url{http://www.khronos.org/opencl/}
	\item {\tt Global Arrays}
	\item {\tt Charm++}
	\item {\tt Cilk}
	\item {\tt CUDA}: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item previously ``Compute Unified Device Architecture''
		\item program in ``C for CUDA'' (C with NVIDIA extensions)
		\item Third party wrappers are also available for: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item {\tt Java}
			\item {\tt Python}
			\item {\tt Fortran}
			\item {\tt Jacket}: A CUDA-engine for MATLAB
			\end{enumerate}
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{enumerate}
\item GPGPU computing vs using multicore/manycore processors: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item options for GPGPU computing include {\tt NVIDIA}'s {\tt CUDA} technology
	\item options for multicore include {\tt IBM}'s {\tt Cell BE}
	\item ``the typical barrier to adopting GPUs is simple, you have to re-write your code''
	\item however, as multi-/many-core processors scale in terms of the number of processor cores, software that run on such multiprocessors need to be rewritten to keep each core busy
	\item note that rewriting code can be very costly in terms of time
	\item rewriting code for GPGPU computing or multi-/many-core processors probably requires software refactoring of the EDA software architecture
	\item explore the benefits of GPGPU computing vs those of multi-/many-core processors the way a software architect would
	\item see \url{http://www.chipdesignmag.com/payne/2009/04/13/gpu-versus-multicore-in-eda/}
	\end{enumerate}
\item techniques to speed up a computer program: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item automatic partitioning
	\item load balancing
	\item event synchronization
	\item memory optimization
	\end{enumerate}
\item {\bf make sure that I provide multi-threading options to my EDA tools}
\item See \url{http://www.cilk.com/} to develop multicore-enabled software applications
\item get compilers that can compile code for parallel computing platforms, such as multicore processors
\item learn dataflow programming, and apply it to concurrent computing to make data flow concurrently
\item learn about data-parallel programming
\item Maurice Herlihy and Nir Shavit, ``The Art of Multiprocessor Programming,'' Morgan Kaufmann Publishers, Burlington, MA, 2008
\\ \rule{6in}{.1pt}

\item {\bf Real-Time Collaboration in Software Development}
\item use real-time collaborative editors so that my teammates and I can edit the same file at the same time; {\it non-real-time collaborative editors do not allow editing of the same file at the same time, so they are similar to revision control systems}
\item for real-time collaboration in software development, try: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item {\tt PaintChats}: facilitate group online chat sessions, and sharing of a whiteboard; see \url{http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paint_chat}
	\item {\it Coccinella}: It allows me to scribble something on a window, my mates' virtual whiteboard, and share my diagrams/words with other people. This is good for collaborating with others in a geographically distributed environment. See \url{http://thecoccinella.org/about}.
	\item {\it Scriblink} ``is a free digital sketchpad that users can share online in real time'': \url{http://www.scriblink.com/}
	\item {\it imagination at work} [General Electric Company]: \url{http://www.imaginationcubed.com/}
	\item {\it TypeWith.me}: \url{http://typewith.me/}
	\item {\it EtherPad} is a ``web-based realtime collaborative document editor'': \url{http://etherpad.com/} and \url{http://code.google.com/p/etherpad/}. Also, see \url{http://ietherpad.com/}.
	\item {\it Etherpad} ``is a hosted web service that allows really real-time document collaboration for groups of users'': \url{http://etherpad.org/}
	\item {\it Sync.in} ``is a web based word processor for people to collaborate in real-time''; this is only free for public documents: \url{http://sync.in/}
	\item {\it PiratePad}: \url{http://piratepad.net/front-page/} and \url{http://piratepad.net/UuYiC8Cy59}
	\item {\it Jarnal}, which ``is an open-source application for notetaking, sketching, keeping a journal, making a presentation, annotating a document - including pdf - or collaborating using a stylus, mouse or keyboard'': \url{http://www.dklevine.com/general/software/tc1000/jarnal.htm} or \url{http://jarnal.wikispaces.com/}. Compare this to ``Microsoft Windows Journal''.
	\item {\it PBworks}, which provides a wiki to support collaboration: \url{http://pbworks.com/}
	\item collaborative real-time editors
	\item computer supported cooperative work (CSCW)
	\item collaborative software (or groupware, or workgroup support systems)
	\item Collaborative Working Environment (CWE) 
	\item {\tt ACE}: a collaborative editor is a platform-independent, collaborative real-time editor; see \url{http://sourceforge.net/projects/ace} 
	\item {\bf Gobby --- free real-time collaborative editor}
	\item {\tt LibreSource}: a collaborative development platform for open-source software, groupware, community interaction, electronic archiving, and Web publishing. See \url{http://dev.libresource.org/}
	\item {\it Dimdim}: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item Is a service that lets anyone host and attend online meetings, demos and webinars via a web browser
		\item Enables documents, web pages, whiteboards, audio, and video to be shared
		\item Enables events to be recorded
		\item Needs no software installation
		\item \url{http://www.dimdim.com/}
		\end{enumerate}
	\item {\it Dropbox}: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item Allows me to share files with others
		\item It also allows others to transfer files to me
		\item \url{https://www.dropbox.com/home#:::}
		\end{enumerate}
	\item {\tt GForge}: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item a free software fork of the web-based project-management and collaboration software
		\item provides facilities for: \vspace{-0.1cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -1pt
			\item Source Code Management (SCM) via CVS and Subversion
			\item customizable Trackers
			\item Task Managers
			\item Document Managers
			\item Forums
			\item Mailing Lists
			\end{enumerate}
		\item \url{http://gforge.org/gf/}
		\end{enumerate}
	\item electronic meeting system (EMS): \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item a type of computer software that facilitates group decision-making within an organization
		\item equivalent to: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item Group Support Systems (GSS)
			\item Group Decision Support Systems (GDSS)
			\end{enumerate}
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{enumerate}

% GUI development
\item {\bf GUI Development}
\item use a GUI builder (graphical user interface builder or GUI designer) to help me create GUIs; examples of such tools are: \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item {\tt Qt} toolkit (pronounced as ``cute'') and {\tt Qt Creator}; see \url{http://qtsoftware.com/}
	\item {\tt GTK+}, or The {\tt GIMP} Toolkit; see \url{http://www.gtk.org/}
	\item {\tt GNUstep}; see \url{http://www.gnustep.org/}
	\item {\tt wxWidgets}; see \url{http://www.wxwidgets.org/}
	\item {\tt Tcl/Tk}; see \url{http://www.tcl.tk/}
	\item {\tt OpenGL} (Open Graphics Library): \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item a standard specification that defines a cross-language, cross-platform API for writing applications that produce 2D and 3D computer graphics
		\item See \url{http://www.khronos.org/opengl/}, \url{http://www.opengl.org/registry/}, and \url{http://www.opengl.org/sdk/}
		\item compare {\tt OpenGL Utility Toolkit (GLUT)} with {\tt freeglut}: \url{http://freeglut.sourceforge.net/}
		\end{enumerate}
	\item {\tt OpenGL User Interface Library (GLUI)}: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item a {\tt C++} user interface library based on the {\tt OpenGL Utility Toolkit (GLUT)}, which provides controls such as buttons, checkboxes, radio buttons, and spinners to {\tt OpenGL} applications
		\item It is window- and operating system independent, relying on {\tt GLUT} to handle all system-dependent issues, such as window and mouse management
		\item It lacks the features of a more full-fledged GUI toolkit such as {\tt Qt}, {\tt wxWidgets}, or {\tt FLTK}, but it has a very small footprint and is extremely easy to use
		\end{enumerate}
	\item {\tt cairo}: 2D graphics library; see \url{http://cairographics.org/}. It seems that {\tt cairo} involves using functional programming to create the graphics/GUI.
	\item {\tt pycairo} is a set of {\tt Python} bindings for the {\tt cairo} graphics library; see \url{http://cairographics.org/pycairo/}
	\item {\tt Motif}; see \url{http://www.openmotif.org/} and \url{http://www.opengroup.org/motif/} --- I do not prefer to use {\tt Motif} because GUI development with {\tt Motif} requires a decent, if not good, Motif GUI IDE. An example of a decent Motif GUI Builder would be the proprietary X-Designer. Note that {\tt Motif} is a proprietary software; the cost of its source code is A LOT (US\$2,000 -- US\$17,000), and its object code costs $\approx$US\$30.
	\item {\tt Interface Builder} (for both {\tt Carbon} and {\tt Cocoa} applications); see \url{http://developer.apple.com/tools/interfacebuilder.html} --- Avoid working with this, since I would be locked in to Apple Inc. and be forced to work on Mac OS X platforms.
	\item {\tt XForms}; see \url{http://savannah.nongnu.org/projects/xforms/}
	\end{enumerate}
\item {\bf Figure out how to develop interactive GUI using} {\bf Java Swing}: \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item I used {\tt Java Swing} to develop the GUI for my honors project at the University of Adelaide, and the simulator for the micromouse project as part of an extracurricular activity with IEEE@USC 
	\item I was unable to use the ``{\tt events manager}'' or threads to handle user activities, and get the GUI to respond appropriately
	\end{enumerate}
\item things that I want to build in my GUI: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item use drag-and-drop, instead of clicking (point-and-click \& double-click) and scrolling in GUI development
	\item pull-down menu or pop-up menu
	\item menu bar
	\item dialog box
	\item tooltip
	\item splash screen 
	\item about box or about dialog
	\end{enumerate}
\item use {\tt ActiveState Komodo} tools, such as {\tt Komodo IDE} and {\tt Komodo Edit}, to develop (web) applications on the Mozilla platform, using dynamic programming languages
