\\ \rule{6in}{.1pt}
% design and analysis of software architecture and algorithms
\item {\bf Design and Analysis of Software Architecture and Algorithms}
\item learn and apply design patterns to software development; use them for: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item object-oriented design (including architectural patterns???)
	\item architectural design: architectural patterns: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item Architectural patterns are software patterns that offer well-established solutions to architectural problems in software engineering.
		\item It gives description of the elements and relation type together with a set of constraints on how they may be used.
		\item An architectural pattern expresses a fundamental structural organization schema for a software system, which consists of subsystems, their responsibilities and interrelations.
		\item In comparison to design patterns, architectural patterns are larger in scale.
		\item Even though an architectural pattern conveys an image of a system, it is not an architecture as such.
		\item An architectural pattern is rather a concept that captures essential elements of a software architecture.
		\item Countless different architectures may implement the same pattern and thereby share the same characteristics.
		\item Furthermore, patterns are often defined as something ``strictly described and commonly available''.
		\item One of the most important aspects of architectural patterns is that they embody different quality attributes. For example, some patterns represent solutions to performance problems and others can be used successfully in high-availability systems. In the early design phase, a software architect makes a choice of which architectural pattern(s) best provide the system's desired qualities.
		\end{enumerate}
	\item fundamental patterns
	\item creational patterns: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item These patterns have to do with class instantiation.
		\item They can be further divided into class-creation patterns and object-creational patterns.
		\item While class-creation patterns use inheritance effectively in the instantiation process, object-creation patterns use delegation to get the job done.
		\item creational design patterns deal with object creation mechanisms to avoid design problems or added design complexity during object creation
		\end{enumerate}
	\item structural patterns: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item These concern class and object composition.
		\item They use inheritance to compose interfaces and define ways to compose objects to obtain new functionality.
		\item structural design patterns are design patterns that ease the design by identifying a simple way to realize relationships between entities
		\end{enumerate}
	\item behavioral patterns: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item Most of these design patterns are specifically concerned with communication between objects.
		\end{enumerate}
	\item concurrency patterns: design patterns that deal with multi-threaded programming paradigm
	\item see ``Design Patterns: Elements of Reusable Object-Oriented Software,'' by Erich Gamma, Richard Helm, Ralph Johnson and John Vlissides (collectively known as the ``Gang of Four'', or GoF for short)
	\end{enumerate}
\rule{6in}{.1pt}
% software development methodology
\item {\bf Software Development Methodology}
\item {\bf software development methodology}: a framework that is used to structure, plan, and control the process of developing software; it is based on a software development philosophy and a set of tools, models, and methods to facilitate software development
\item $\nexists$ a software development methodology that can be suitably used by all projects
\item a software development methodology shall be chosen based on various technical, organizational, project, and team considerations
\item familiarize myself with various software development processes and methodologies, such as: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -3pt
	\item object oriented development methodologies, including: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item object-oriented analysis and design (OOAD)
		\item object-oriented programming
		\end{enumerate}
	\item agile methodologies: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item Extreme Programming / eXtreme Programming (XP):
		\item Scrum
		\item Agile Unified Process (AUP)
		\end{enumerate}
	\item heavyweight software development processes: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item Rational Unified Process (RUP)
		\item cleanroom software engineering
		\end{enumerate}
	\item generic software development approaches: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item waterfall: linear framework type
		\item prototyping: iterative framework type
		\item incremental: combination of linear and iterative framework types
		\item spiral: combination of linear and iterative framework types
		\item rapid application development (RAD): iterative framework type
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{enumerate}
\item software development life cycle (SDLC): \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item phases in the SDLC: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item {\it initiation}
		\item {\it system concept development}
		\item planning
		\item $[$requirements gathering and$]$ analysis
		\item design
		\item {\it development} $[$building and/or coding$]$
		\item {\it integration and test}
		\item implementation
		\item $[$operations and$]$ maintenance
		\item {\it deployment / installation / disposition}
		\item {\it iterate the process}
		\end{enumerate}
	\item management control domains: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item planning and organization: project definition; user requirements definition; and system requirements definition
		\item acquisition and implementation: user requirements definition; system requirements definition; analysis and design; and system build/prototype/pilot
		\item delivery and support: analysis and design; system build/prototype/pilot; implementation and training; and sustainment
		\item monitoring: project definition; user requirements definition; system requirements definition; analysis and design; system build/prototype/pilot; implementation and training; and sustainment
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{enumerate}
\item SDLC models: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item sequential or big-design-upfront (BDUF) models: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
		\item Waterfall model: Stepwise refinement; also see ``Sashimi model'' (modified waterfall model)
		\end{itemize}
	\item iterative and incremental development (IID): \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
		\item agile software development: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
			\item eXtreme Programming (XP) --- see elaboration below
			\item Scrum
			\item Crystal Clear
			\item Feature Driven Development (FDD)
			\item values captured by the Agile Manifesto: \vspace{-0.2cm}
				\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
				\item {\tt individuals and interactions} over processes and tools
				\item {\tt working software} over comprehensive documentation
				\item {\tt customer collaboration} over contract negotiation
				\item {\tt responding to change} over following a plan
				\end{itemize}
			\item principles behind the Agile Manifesto: \vspace{-0.2cm}
				\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
				\item customer satisfaction by rapid, continuous delivery of useful software
				\item working software is delivered frequently (weeks rather than months)
				\item working software is the principal measure of progress
				\item even late changes in requirements are welcomed (this does not mean code and run. Instead removing an existing feature or moving a deadline forward to accommodate late/unplanned feature requests)
				\item close, daily cooperation between business people and developers
				\item face-to-face conversation is the best form of communication (Co-location)
				\item projects are built around motivated individuals, who should be trusted
				\item continuous attention to technical excellence and good design
				\item simplicity
				\item self-organizing teams
				\item regular adaptation to changing circumstances
				\end{itemize}
			\item Agile Modeling (AM): \url{http://www.agilemodeling.com/}
			\end{itemize}
		\item Spiral model: incremental model
		\item Rational Unified Process (RUP), which is a subset of the Unified Software Development Process (or Unified Process)
		\item lean software development
		\item Goal-Driven Software Development Process (GDP)
		\item Adaptive Software Development
		\item {\it Wheel And Spoke Model} (sort of)
		\item From Toyota Production System (TPS): Muda, : \vspace{-0.1cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -1pt
			\item Muda is a traditional Japanese term for an activity that is wasteful and doesn't add value or is unproductive, etymologically none or un-useful in practice or others.
			\item Mura is traditional general Japanese term for unevenness, irregularity, or inconsistency in physical matter or human spiritual condition.
			\item Muri (unreasonable) is a Japanese term for overburden, unreasonableness, or absurdity. Muri can be avoided through standardized work.
			\item Three types of waste associated with TPS: muda, mura, and muri.
			\item Also, see lean manufacturing, lean software development, and total quality management (TQM).
			\end{enumerate}
		\end{itemize}
	\item rapid application development (RAD); include the derivative ``Dynamic Systems Development Method (DSDM)''; that is, DSDM is based on RAD
	\item V-model: also see Dual Vee Model
	\item Chaos model: Chaos strategy (extends the stepwise refinement strategy)
	\end{enumerate}
\item eXtreme Programming (XP): \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item Compare this with ``flexible product development'' outside the software industry
	\item goals of XP: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -1pt
		\item reduce the cost of change (the main aim of XP)
		\item An attempt to reconcile humanity and productivity
		\item A mechanism for social change
		\item A path to improvement
		\item A style of development
		\item A software development discipline
		\end{enumerate}
	\item values of XP: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -1pt
		\item Communication
		\item Simplicity
		\item Feedback
		\item Courage
		\item Respect
		\end{enumerate}
	\item XP is defined by its rules, rather than just by its rules and practices: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -1pt
		\item some XP practices are fuzzy in definition
		\item you can be doing XP without following all the practices
		\item the Rules of Play are what make XP unique
		\item following the Rules of Play is Extreme Programming
		\item following the Rules of Play and the Rules of Engagement is Extreme Software Development
		\end{enumerate}
	\item rules of engagement: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -1pt
		\item collaboration between business people and developers: they must work together daily throughout the project
		\item primary priority is customer satisfaction : the customer must set and continuously adjust the objectives and priorities based on estimates, and other information provided by the developers (or other members) of the team; objectives are defined in terms of what must be accomplished, not how will things be accomplished
		\item deliver working software frequently: the frequency can range in the order of several weeks/months; preferences are given to the shorter time scale (Timeboxing)
		\item working software: the primary measure of progress
		\item global awareness: At any point, any member of the team must be able to measure the teamÕs progress towards the customerÕs objectives and the team reflects on how to become more effective, then tunes and adjusts its behavior accordingly
		\item the team must act as an {\bf effective social network}, which means: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item honest communication leading to continuous learning and an emphasis on person-to-person interaction, rather than documentation
			\item minimal degrees of separation from what is needed by the team to make progress and the people/resources that can meet those needs
			\item alignment of authority and responsibility
			\end{enumerate}
		\end{enumerate}
	\item rules of play: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -1pt
		\item {\bf continuous testing}: work produced must be continuously validated/by testing
		\item {\bf clearness and quality of code}: all code written for potential use in the software product must clearly express every concept and have clarity, contain no duplication and no superfluous parts and pass all the unit tests.
		\item {\bf common vocabulary}: there is a sketch of the product guide all development activities with a simple shared story of how the whole system works; so everyone involved could grasp the essence of the project in a term universally understood
		\item {\bf everybody has the authority}: everybody has the authority to do any task, and at least two people have the understanding necessary to do any task
		\end{enumerate}
	\item principles that form the basis of XP: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -1pt
		\item feedback
		\item assume simplicity 
		\item embrace change
		\end{enumerate}
	\item practices of XP: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -1pt
		\item fine scale feedback: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item pair programming
			\item planning game
			\item test-driven development
			\item whole team
			\end{enumerate}
		\item continuous process: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item continuous integration
			\item refactoring or design improvement
			\item small releases
			\end{enumerate}
		\item shared understanding: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item coding standards
			\item collective code ownership
			\item simple design
			\item system metaphor
			\end{enumerate}
		\item programmer welfare: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item sustainable pace
			\end{enumerate}
		\item modularity (facilitate refactoring and simplify design)
		\item bottom-up (unit testing, module testing, integration testing, and automated regression testing)
		\item incremental design (regular builds/releases)
		\end{enumerate}
	\item resources \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item \url{http://princetonacm.acm.org/downloads/extreme_agile.html}
		\item \url{http://xprogramming.com}
		\item \url{http://www.extremeprogramming.org/}
		\item \url{http://c2.com/cgi/wiki?ExtremeProgramming}
		\item \url{http://www.industrialxp.org/}
		\item \url{http://ootips.org/xp.html}
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{enumerate}
\item software prototyping: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item throwaway/rapid prototyping, or close ended prototyping: costly activity, prototype is not used again, or to facilitate the development of the software product
	\item evolutionary/breadboard prototyping
	\end{enumerate}
\item process models: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item CMMI (and CMM): \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item Capability Maturity Model Integration (CMMI)'s areas of interest: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item CMMI for Development (CMMI-DEV): Product and service development
			\item CMMI for Services (CMMI-SVC): Service establishment, management, and delivery
			\item CMMI for Acquisition (CMMI-ACQ): Product and service acquisition
			\end{enumerate}
		\item levels of process maturity (in CMMI) for an organization: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item Level 5: {\tt Optimizing}: \vspace{-0.2cm}
				\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
				\item Focus: continuous process improvement
				\item process areas: \vspace{-0.2cm}
					\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
					\item organizational innovation and deployment
					\item causal analysis and resolution
					\end{itemize}
				\end{itemize}
			\item Level 4: {\tt Quantitatively Managed}: \vspace{-0.2cm}
				\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
				\item Focus: quantitative management
				\item process areas: \vspace{-0.2cm}
					\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
					\item organizational process performance
					\item quantitative project management
					\end{itemize}
				\end{itemize}
			\item Level 3: {\tt Defined}: \vspace{-0.2cm}
				\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
				\item Focus: process standardization
				\item process areas: \vspace{-0.2cm}
					\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
					\item requirements development
					\item technical solution
					\item product integration
					\item verification
					\item validation
					\item organizational process focus
					\item organizational process definition
					\item organizational training
					\item integrated project management
					\item risk management
					\item decision analysis and resolution
					\end{itemize}
				\end{itemize}
			\item Level 2: {\tt Managed}: \vspace{-0.2cm}
				\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
				\item Focus: basic project management
				\item process areas: \vspace{-0.2cm}
					\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
					\item requirements management
					\item project planning
					\item project monitoring and control
					\item supplier agreement and management
					\item management and analysis
					\item process and product quality assurance
					\item configuration management
					\end{itemize}
				\end{itemize}
			\item Level 1: {\tt Initial}: \vspace{-0.2cm}
				\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
				\item Focus: competent people and heroics
				\item competent people and heroics
				\end{itemize}
			\end{enumerate}
		\item levels of process maturity (in CMM) for an organization: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item Initial ({\tt ad hoc}, {\tt chaotic}, heroic) the starting point for use of a new process --- (Level 1)
			\item {\tt Repeatable} (project management, process discipline) the process is used repeatedly --- (Level 2)
			\item {\tt Defined} (institutionalized) the process is defined/confirmed as a standard business process --- (Level 3)
			\item {\tt Managed} (quantified) process management and measurement takes place --- (Level 4)
			\item {\tt Optimizing} (process improvement) process management includes deliberate process optimization/improvement --- (Level 5)
			\end{enumerate}
		\item Key Process Areas (KPAs) for characterizing CMM maturity level: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item Goals
			\item Commitment
			\item Ability
			\item Measurement
			\item Verification
			\end{enumerate}
		\end{enumerate}
	\item ISO/IEC 15504, also known as SPICE (Software Process Improvement and Capability Determination): \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item capability levels and process attributes: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item Optimizing process (Level 5)
			\item Predictable process (Level 4)
			\item Established process (Level 3)
			\item Managed process (Level 2)
			\item Performed process (Level 1)
			\item Incomplete process (Level 0)
			\end{enumerate}
		\item process dimension: process categories \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item engineering
			\item management
			\item organizational
			\item customer-supplier and/or acquisition supply
			\item support
			\item operations
			\end{enumerate}
		\item process attributes: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item Process Performance
			\item Performance Management
			\item Work Product Management
			\item Process Definition
			\item Process Deployment
			\item Process Measurement
			\item Process Control
			\item Process Innovation
			\item Process Optimization
			\item four-point (N-P-L-F) rating scale for process attributes: \vspace{-0.2cm}
				\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
				\item Not achieved (0 - 15\%)
				\item Partially achieved ($>$15\% - 50\%)
				\item Largely achieved ($>$50\%- 85\%)
				\item Fully achieved ($>$85\% - 100\%)
				\end{itemize}
			\end{enumerate}
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{enumerate}
\rule{6in}{.1pt}
% programming paradigm
\item {\bf Programming Paradigm}
\item use declarative programming to improve the productivity of software development
\\ \rule{6in}{.1pt}
% advice and tips on computer programming
\item {\bf Advice and Tips on Computer Programming}
\item when writing computer programs, place the constants on the left-hand side (LHS) when comparing a variable and a constant; this will avoid confusion with assignment statements, where the constants are always on the right-hand side (RHS)
\\ \rule{6in}{.1pt}
% software release life cycle and project life cycle
\item {\bf Project Life Cycle and Software Release Life Cycle}
\item product life cycle phase and project life cycle: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item inception
	\item first development
	\item major release
	\item minor release
	\item bug fix release
	\item maintenance
	\item obsolescence
	\end{enumerate}
\item software release life cycle: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item software release life cycle: composed of different stages that describe the stability of a piece of software, and the amount as the development process proceeds
	\item software release: the distribution (whether public or private) of an initial or upgraded version of a computer software product
	\item release stages of software development (in the development and testing period): \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item Pre-alpha release: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item nightly builds
			\item development release
			\end{enumerate}
		\item release development stage, near the end of a release cycle: \vspace{-0.3cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item stability of code: \vspace{-0.2cm}
				\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
				\item unstable release
				\item stable release
				\end{itemize}
			\item Alpha release; e.g., 3.0.a, 3.0-a, 3.0.0
			\item Beta release: \vspace{-0.2cm}
				\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
				\item closed beta
				\item open beta
				\item Beta 1 release; e.g., 3.0.b1, 3.0.b, 3.0-b1, 3.0.1
				\item Beta 2 release (with some bug fixes); e.g., 3.0.b2, 3.0.b2, 3.0-b2
				\item {\it Beta 3 release}
				\end{itemize}
			\item release candidate (RC): \vspace{-0.1cm}
				\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
				\item Gamma release; e.g., 5.0rc1, 5.0-rc, 5.0.2
				\item Delta release; e.g., 5.0rc2, 5.0-rc2
				\item ... till {\it Omega release}
				\item {\tt Gold master (GM) release / GM seed} -- Apple, Inc.
				\item A release is called code complete when the development team agrees that no entirely new source code will be added to this release: \vspace{-0.2cm}
					\begin{itemize} \itemsep -2pt
					\item There may still be source code changes to fix defects
					\item There may still be changes to documentation and data files, and to the code for test cases or utilities
					\item New code may be added in a future release
					\end{itemize}
				\end{itemize}
			\item {\it RTM: Release to Marketing / Release to Manufacturing}
			\item {\it General Availability}
			\item {\it production release, live release, commercial distribution, or Gold release}; e.g., 5.0r, 5.0-r, 5.0.3
			\item commercial distribution with many bug fixes; e.g., 5.2r5, 5.2-r5
			\item 1.0, 2.0, 3.0, ... ; X.0, where X $\in \mathbb{N} = \{1, 2, 3, ...\}$
			\item Version numbers (sequence-based identifiers for software versioning): \vspace{-0.2cm}
				\begin{itemize} \itemsep -2pt
				\item sequence-based identifiers for software versioning: each software release is assigned a unique identifier that consists of one or more sequences of numbers or letters
				\item schemes vary widely in areas such as:: \vspace{-0.2cm}
					\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
					\item the quantity of sequences
					\item the attribution of meaning to individual sequences
					\item the means of incrementing the sequences
					\end{itemize}
				\item examples of schemes: \vspace{-0.2cm}
					\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
					\item major.minor$[$.build$[$.revision$]]$
					\item major.minor$[$.maintenance$[$.build$]]$
					\end{itemize}
				\item increment the first number ({\bf major number}), when significant changes are made to the features of the software
				\item increment the second number ({\bf minor number}) for minor changes: \vspace{-0.2cm}
					\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
					\item even second number are stable releases
					\item odd second number are unstable releases
					\item distribute release notes with these releases; see \url{http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Release_notes}
					\end{itemize}
				\item increment the third number ({\bf revision number}) for: \vspace{-0.2cm}
					\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
					\item bug fixes
					\item change to the user interface (UI)
					\item change to the documentation
					\end{itemize}
				\item increment the fourth number ({\bf trivial version number}) for {\tt very minor changes}
				\item Other software versioning schemes: \vspace{-0.2cm}
					\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
					\item 
					\end{itemize}
				\end{itemize} 
			\end{enumerate}
		\end{enumerate}
	\item In software development, a rolling release approach refers to a continuously developing software system, as opposed to one with versions that must be reinstalled over the previous versions... A rolling release is typically implemented using small and frequent updates.
	\end{enumerate}
\rule{6in}{.1pt}
% software portability
\item {\bf Software Portability}
\item for {\tt Ruby} programs, Jruby is a compiler that can generate Java bytecode; likewise, Jython can do the same for Python programs; this is because Java bytecode instructions are machine independent, while conventional processor dependent machine code isn't
\item write cross-platform (or multi-platform) software: write once, compile anywhere (WOCA)
\\ \rule{6in}{.1pt}
% software metrics
\item {\bf Software Metrics}
\item learn to develop metrics for your goals and objectives, determine the error tolerance, make appropriate trade-offs, and measure them
\\ \rule{6in}{.1pt}
% software engineering practices
\item {\bf Software Engineering Practices}
\item pair programming
\item programming productivity
\item software reuse
\item systems integration
\\ \rule{6in}{.1pt}
% software engineering resources
\item {\bf Software Engineering Resources}
\item software engineering textbooks: Stephen R. (Steve) Schach, Ian Sommerville, Roger Pressman
\\ \rule{6in}{.1pt}
% Guidelines for Algorithm Design
\item {\bf Guidelines for Algorithm Design}
\item Whenever you are asked to design a new algorithm, there are a number of things you should do. This document itemizes your responsibilities as an algorithm designer. In particular, you are expected to do each of the following tasks whenever writing up a solution to design problem in CMSC-441 or CMSC-641: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item Give your algorithm a name.
	\item State the input/output specifications of the algorithm.
	\item Write your algorithm in clear HIGH-LEVEL pseudocode. Your pseudocode should be detailed enough that a programmer could easily translate it into a high-level programming language; however, your pseudocode should not be so detailed as to look like low-level C code. Write your code as clearly and in as high a level as possible.
	\item Briefly and intuitively explain how your algorithm works. State the design strategy; do not explain your pseudocode line-by-line.
	\item Prove that your algorithm works correctly.
	\item State and prove the resource usage of your algorithm, including its running time and memory usage.
	\item State and prove any significant special properties of your algorithm.
	\item Extra notes: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item Justify the steps/approach of my algorithm. Why did I do that? How is this beneficial?
		\item Elementary steps should be used in the pseudocode. Complex sequences of elementary steps should be wrapped as functional calls.
		\item Describe unambiguously each procedure with a finite number of steps to solve my research problems.
		\item Use examples to adequately explain my proposed techniques.
		\end{enumerate}
	\item Alan T. Sherman, ``Algorithm's Policy,'' {\it Prof. Alan Theodore Sherman's web page: Courses: How To's and Other Generic Course Documents}, Department of Computer Science and Electrical Engineering, University of Maryland, Baltimore County, February 15, 1997. Reference: Available online at: \url{http://www.csee.umbc.edu/~sherman/Courses/documents/algorithms_policy.html}; last accessed on February 8, 2012.
	\end{enumerate}
\rule{6in}{.1pt}
% general advice and random notes
\item {\bf General Advice and Random Notes}
\item learn how to write production-quality code, and develop industrial-grade EDA tools: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -3pt
	\item What is production-quality code?
	\item How do I write production quality code?
	\item What makes an EDA tool industrial-grade?
	\item How do I develop industrial-grade EDA tools?
	\item e.g., develop ``leading-edge industrial-grade VLSI Physical Design tools''; see \url{http://vlsicad.eecs.umich.edu/BK/PDtools/}; last viewed June 21, 2009 @ 0645 hrs
	\end{enumerate}
\item keep working on improving my development speed: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -3pt
	\item the faster I can develop software, the faster I can try and implement new algorithms/heuristics to solve research problems, and the more publications I can churn out
	\item also, development speed is dependent on the choice of computer language used: \vspace{-0.3cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -3pt
		\item programming languages: \vspace{-0.1cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item {\tt C++}
			\item {\tt Java}\texttrademark
			\item {\tt MATLAB}\textregistered
			\item {\tt C\#}
			\item {\tt Fortran} (previously {\tt FORTRAN})
			\item {\tt C}
			\item \MP
			\item visual programming language (VPL): \vspace{-0.2cm}
				\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
				\item {\tt Simulink}\textregistered
				\item {\tt LabVIEW}\texttrademark
				\end{itemize}
			\item assembly language: \vspace{-0.2cm}
				\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
				\item {\tt MIPS}
				\item {\tt DLX}
				\item {\tt Motorola 68000} / Motorola 68k / Motorola 680x0
				\end{itemize}
			\item curly bracket programming languages
			\end{enumerate}
		\item scripting languages: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item {\tt Ruby}
			\item {\tt Python}: off-side rule
			\item {\tt Perl}
			\item {\tt Tcl} (Tool Command Language)
			\item {\tt UNIX shell scripts}: \vspace{-0.2cm}
				\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
				\item {\tt Bash}: Bourne-again shell
				\item {\tt Bourne shell (sh)}
				\item {\tt C shell (csh)}
				\item {\tt tcsh}
				\end{itemize}
			\item {\tt AWK}
			\item {\tt sed}
			\item {\tt PHP}
			\item {\tt SenseTalk}\texttrademark
			\item {\tt AppleScript}\textregistered
			\end{enumerate}
		\item hardware description languages (HDLs): \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item {\tt Verilog}
			\item {\tt Verilog-AMS}
			\item {\tt SystemVerilog}\texttrademark
			\item {\tt SystemC}\texttrademark
			\item {\tt SystemC-AMS}\texttrademark
			\item {\tt VHDL}
			\item {\tt VHDL-AMS}
			\item See the respective Language Reference Manuals (LRMs)s for more details of these HDLs
			\end{enumerate}
		\item query languages: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item {\tt SQL}
			\end{enumerate}
		\item modeling languages: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item {\tt UML}, which is also a specification language and architecture description language, ADL
			\end{enumerate}
		\item markup language: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item \LaTeX
			\item {\tt HTML}
			\item {\tt XML} (Extensible Markup Language)
			\end{enumerate}
		\item declarative language: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item {\tt OCL}: Object Constraint Language
			\end{enumerate}
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{enumerate}
\item use macros to speed things up; write/develop them for my text editor and/or my (computer) program
\item use {\tt Automator} on {\tt Mac OS}\textregistered\ X to implement point-and-click (or drag-and-drop) creation of sequential workflows for automating repetitive tasks: \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item Automator enables the repetition of tasks across a wide variety of programs
	\item Automator uses {\tt AppleScript}\textregistered\ and {\tt Cocoa}\textregistered
	\end{enumerate}
\item How should I measure programming productivity? \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item amount of code that can be created or maintained per programmer (often measured in KLOCs per day)
	\item detecting and avoiding errors (through techniques like Six Sigma management, Zero Defects coding, and Total Quality Management)
	\item software cost estimation (cost being a direct consequence of productivity)
	\end{enumerate}
\item AVOID the use of anti-patterns (or antipatterns) AT ALL COSTS
\item never ever knowingly join a death march project: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item In the software development and software engineering industries, a death march is a dysphemism for a project that is destined to fail.
	\item Usually it is a result of unrealistic or overly optimistic expectations in scheduling, feature scope, or both, and often includes lack of appropriate documentation, or any sort of relevant training.
	\item The knowledge of the doomed nature of the project weighs heavily on the psyche of its participants, as if they are helplessly watching the team as it marches into the sea.
	\item Often, the death march will involve desperate attempts to right the course of the project by asking team members to work especially grueling hours, weekends, or by attempting to ``throw (enough) bodies at the problem'' with varying results, often causing burnout.
	\end{enumerate}
\item Brooks's law is a principle in software development which says that ``adding manpower to a late software project makes it later'': \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item ``ramp up'' time: It takes some time for the people added to a project to become productive
	\item Communication overheads increase as the number of people increase
	\end{enumerate}
\item detect {\bf code smells}, and make appropriate corrections promptly
\item avoid ``development hell,'' where the software is stuck in the implementation phrase for a considerably long time; if I am in charge if such a project, should it be shelved?
\item beware of the {\bf software Peter principle}: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item The software Peter principle is used in software engineering to describe a dying project which has little by little become too complex to be understood even by its own developers.
	\item It is well known in the industry as a silent killer of projects, and by the time the symptoms arise it is often too late to do anything about it.
	\item Good managers can avoid this disaster by establishing clear coding practices where unnecessarily complicated code and design is avoided.
	\item The Peter Principle is the principle that ``In a Hierarchy, Every Employee Tends to Rise to His Level of Incompetence.''
	\end{enumerate}
\item The Dilbert Principle refers to a 1990s satirical observation by Dilbert cartoonist Scott Adams stating that companies tend to systematically promote their least-competent employees to management (generally middle management), in order to limit the amount of damage they're capable of doing: \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item In the Dilbert strip of February 5, 1995 Dogbert says that ``leadership is nature's way of removing morons from the productive flow.''
	\end{enumerate}
\item Parkinson's Law is the adage first articulated by Cyril Northcote Parkinson as the first sentence of a humorous essay published in The Economist in 1955: Work expands so as to fill the time available for its completion. 
\item Parkinson's Law of Triviality (also known as the bicycle shed example, and by the expression colour of the bikeshed) is C. Northcote Parkinson's 1957 argument that organisations give disproportionate weight to trivial issues.
\item Student syndrome refers to the phenomenon that many people will start to fully apply themselves to a task just at the last possible moment before a deadline. This leads to wasting any buffers built into individual task duration estimates.
\item reasons to rewrite a function/method, or class: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item When the source code to be able to extend an existing program is not available.
	\item When the source code is available under an incompatible license.
	\item When the code cannot be adapted to a new target platform.
	\item When the existing code has become too difficult to handle and extend.
	\item When the task of debugging the existing code seems too complicated.
	\item When the programmer finds it difficult to understand the source code.
	\end{enumerate}
\item the contents of a readme (or read me) file may contain: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item configuration instructions
	\item installation instructions; can be placed in the file {\tt install.txt}
	\item operating instructions
	\item a file manifest
	\item copyright and licensing information; can be placed in the file {\tt license.txt}
	\item contact information for the distributor or programmer
	\item known bugs; can be placed in the file {\tt bugs.txt}
	\item troubleshooting
	\item credits; can be placed in the file {\tt authors.txt}
	\item acknowledgments; can be placed in the file {\tt thanks.txt}
	\item a changelog; can be placed in the file {\tt news.txt} or {\tt changelog.txt}
	\end{enumerate}
\item Prefactoring is the application of past experience to the creation of new software systems. Its relationship to its namesake refactoring is that lessons learned from refactoring are part of that experience.
\item for each software I develop/co-develop, consider providing support for encryption/decryption of source files, and other files
\item goals of each software project: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item high quality software
	\item meet or exceed customer expectations
	\item reach completion $[$of the project$]$ within time and cost estimates
	\item work effectively and efficiently in the current and planned Information Technology infrastructure
	\item is inexpensive to maintain
	\item cost-effective to enhance
	\end{enumerate}
\item become a subject matter expert: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item A Subject Matter Expert (SME) is a person who is an expert in a particular area.
	\item In software engineering environments, the term is used to describe professionals with expertise in the field of application but without technical project knowledge.
	\end{enumerate}
\rule{6in}{.1pt}
% checklists
\item {\bf Checklists}
\item {\bf When comparing my formal verification (or EDA) tools/solutions to others, do other tools/solutions provide comparable or better capabilities?:} \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item {\bf Do others need a set of tools to mach what my tool can do?}
	\item {\bf Do my tools have better performance?}
	\item {\bf Do my tools have better memory capacity/usage?}
	\item {\bf Can my tools handle bigger inputs (such as design models or netlists)?}
	\item {\bf Do my tools scale better for a bigger parallel computing platform?}
	\end{enumerate}
\end{enumerate}




%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\subsection{\hspace{0.1in} Important Considerations for Developing EDA Tools}
\label{imptconsiderationsedaswdev}


Important considerations for developing EDA tools: \vspace{-0.3cm}
\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -4pt
\item How can I turn on/off different parts of the code? \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item Can I use this to facilitate debugging? In particularly, I want to know if I can use different namespaces for different parts of a C++ program to facilitate debugging.
	\end{enumerate}
\item Within a C++ class, use: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item Copy constructor \cite[pp. 5-6 of the Introduction]{Meyers2005}: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item Initialize object with a different object of the same type
		\item Widget(const Widget\& rhs);
		\item When called, we are ``passing-by-value''
		\end{enumerate}
	\item Copy assignment operator \cite[pp. 5-6 of the Introduction]{Meyers2005}: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item Copy the value from one object to another object of the same type
		\item Widget\& operator=(const Widget\& rhs);
		\item When called, we ``pass-by-reference-to-const''
		\end{enumerate}
	\rule{6in}{.1pt}
	\item Function objects \cite[pp. 6 of the Introduction]{Meyers2005}: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item Objects that act like functions
		\item Overload the function call operator {\tt operator()} in the class definition
		\end{enumerate}
	\item Function pointers
	\\ \rule{6in}{.1pt}
	\item Inline function \cite[Chapter 1, pp. 16--17]{Meyers2005}: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item Has the efficiency of C/C++ macros
		\item Has predictable behavior
		\item Has type safety of regular function
		\item Parameters of the function would not be evaluated multiple times
		\item Parameters inside the function body need not be parenthesized
		\item It obeys scope and access rules
		\item {\it inline void callWithMax(const T{\rm \&} a, const T{\rm \&} b) \{ f(a $>$ b ? a : b); \}	}
		\end{enumerate}
	\item The {\tt const} keyword can be applied to \cite[Chapter 1, pp. 17]{Meyers2005}: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item outside of classes: \vspace{-0.1cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -1pt
			\item constants at global or namespace scope
			\item objects that are declared static at file, function, or block scope
			\end{enumerate}
		\item inside classes: \vspace{-0.1cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -1pt
			\item static data members, and pointers to these members
			\item non-static data members, and pointers to these members
			\item within a function declaration \cite[Chapter 1, pp. 18]{Meyers2005}: \vspace{-0.1cm}
				\begin{itemize} \itemsep -1pt
				\item A function's return value
				\item Individual parameters: pass-by-reference-to-const, or pass-by-pointer-to-const
				\item The entire function (for member functions); i.e., const member functions \cite[Chapter 1, pp. 19]{Meyers2005} --- For bitwise constness (don't modify any of the object's non-static data members-here, object refers to an instance of the class that the function belongs to); and for logical constness \cite[Chapter 1, pp. 21]{Meyers2005}
				\end{itemize}
			\end{enumerate}
		\item The {\tt const} keyword can be listed before or after the data type; in either case, they have the same meaning \cite[Chapter 1, pp. 18]{Meyers2005}
		\item iterators (i.e., {\tt const\_iterator}) -- Iterators that point to things that can't be modified \cite[Chapter 1, pp. 18]{Meyers2005}
		\end{enumerate}
	\rule{6in}{.1pt}
	\item Template metaprogramming (TMP) \cite[Chapter 7, pp. 233--238, Item 48]{Meyers2005}
	\\ \rule{6in}{.1pt}
	\item \cite{Stroustrup2000}
	\item More Effective C++ \cite{Meyers1996}
	\item STL \cite{Meyers2001}
	\end{enumerate}
\end{enumerate}



%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\subsection{\hspace{0.1in} Notes on VLSI Design}
\label{notesonvlsidesign}

Notes on VLSI design: \vspace{-0.3cm}
\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -4pt
\item using VIM for {\tt Verilog}, {\tt C}, {\tt Perl}, {\tt csh/tcsh}, \& {\tt Tcl}; see \url{http://www-vlsi.stanford.edu/~jsolomon/vim/} 
\item {\tt Perl} scripts to drive {\tt Synopsys} tools; see \url{http://www-vlsi.stanford.edu/~jsolomon/SPP/}
\item learn how to use and tweak configuration management tools, such as {\tt CVS}, {\tt Subversion}, and {\tt Git}, for VLSI design management: \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -3pt
	\item configuration management tools are also known as: \vspace{-0.3cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item revision control system
		\item version control system
		\item source control system
		\item (source) code management (SCM) system
		\end{enumerate}
	\item design management involves and contains: \vspace{-0.3cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item change management
		\item handling of Engineering Change Orders (ECOs), including: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -1pt
			\item bug reports
			\item feature requests
			\item database for all filed ECOs and processed ECOs
			\item corrected faults/bugs
			\end{enumerate}
		\item a framework that supports all the EDA tools that are used by the IC design, verification, testing, and validation teams 
		\item netlists, source code (e.g., HDL, test benches, \& HVL), simulation scripts (e.g., {\tt SPICE} deck, {\tt IRSIM} test bench), system-level models (@ the architectural- or microarchitectural-level)
		\item project documentation: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -1pt
			\item design document: including architectural and microarchitectural designs, RTL/logic designs (schematic), \& floorplan
			\item verification plan
			\item testing plan
			\item post-silicon validation plan
			\item user manual for chipset / chip specification document
			\item project management plan: e.g., timeline and/or Gantt chart
			\item scripts that glue the tools together, and scripts that are used to drive/run the tools
			\item scripts that are used to manipulate source code and netlists: e.g., parsers
			\item versions and setup environment of EDA tools
			\end{enumerate}
		\item bug/fault tracking: track the type, severity, and number of bugs/faults
		\item different builds, subversions, and versions of design, verification, and simulation files, as well as project documentation
		\end{enumerate}
	\item companies that provide VLSI (or IC) ``design management systems'' include: IC Manage and Cliosoft
	\end{enumerate}
\end{enumerate}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\subsection{\hspace{0.1in} Note on Using the OpenAccess SDK by Donald Chai}
\label{openaccesssdknotedonaldchai}

A while ago, I needed to use the OpenAccess SDK to write some code to do some physical design. I tried to get the much touted Python wrappers to work, but they were for OA2.1, while I was using OA2.1.1. The OA API changed just enough to foil any attempts at compilation. Since those wrappers were a big monolith of generated code, I figured I'd probably be better off writing my own. \\
\ \\
I chose SWIG over Boost.Python because of the more advanced typemaps. I actually like Boost.Python better, because it avoids shadow classes, but SWIG's typemaps seemed like the right thing for wrapping someone else's code. Not all of the API is wrapped, because I didn't need all of the API. (Finishing it is left as an exercise to the reader.)\\
\ \\
-- Donald Chai; then a EECS Ph.D. candidate @ UC Berkeley under the supervision of Prof. Andreas Kuehlmann; see \url{http://www.cs.berkeley.edu/~donald/code/pyoa/}; last viewed in mid 2009



%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\subsection{\hspace{0.1in} Publishing Research Papers in EDA}
\label{publishingresearchpapersineda}

Publishing research papers in EDA: \vspace{-0.3cm}
\begin{itemize} \itemsep -4pt
\item use DUDE to check for the amount of new material for publication; see \url{http://ariane4.eecs.umich.edu/DUDE/}
\item join/create reading groups for reviewing and discussing journal and conference papers related to my research area; e.g., reading groups for VLSI formal verification, SMT/SAT, MPSoC, \& NoC
\item Pat Hanrahan says: ``Future Work section must be earned''.  If you haven't made us care about your contribution thus far, we won't care to read {\tt Future Work} either.
\item analog formal verification: Quo Vadis? --- Where are we going with analog formal verification? What are the research directions for analog formal verification?
\item {\bf When writing journal and conference papers, turn your shortcomings into advantages. For example, ``while it cannot do Task A, it is acknowledged that Task B is more important than Task A. So, it doesn't matter if Task A can be done very well, or done at all.'' }
\item Nature Publishing Group (a division of Macmillan Publishers Limited): \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item Kendall Powell, ``Publications: Publish like a pro,'' in {\it Nature}, Vol. 467, No. 7317, pp. 873--875, October 14, 2010. Available online at: \url{http://dx.doi.org/10.1038/nj7317-873a}; last accessed on December 31, 2010. \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{itemize} \itemsep -2pt
		\item Find out how do prolific researchers get their manuscripts noticed, reviewed, and approved for publication
		\item Take rejection of your journal/conference paper submission ``at face value.''
		\item Prolific researchers get more papers published, since they are more persistent in trying to get the paper published.
		\item ``If you've got the gut feeling that you've got good stuff, you've just got to be persistent.'' -- Matt Rayner
		\item ``The refereeing process can be haphazard'' -- Mark Hauber
		\item ``The moral of the story is that the publishing process requires not only hard work but also resilience -- and struggling young authors can learn valuable lessons from those who have already navigated that process.''
		\item Quote: In taking the all-important route to publication, inexperienced authors have several factors to consider and obstacles to overcome, from finding ways to combat writer's block to gracefully pursuing the journal-submission and review process. Established authors and journal editors suggest thinking early about the right journal and finding an appropriate editor, the best reviewers and, of course, an appropriate audience of readers. And new authors should be careful to polish their work and respond meticulously and politely to reviewers' comments without getting overwhelmed or frustrated by lengthy, time-consuming queries. Those who follow such advice are more likely to find success. Those who don't could end up on the wrong side of the `publish or perish' divide.
		\item Quote: For many publishing veterans, the writing process starts at the earliest stages of designing a research project. ``Nothing beats a comprehensive, thought-out experiment. Do that up front and your writing will come so much more easily,'' says Mark Blumberg, a neuroscientist at the University of Iowa in Iowa City and editor-in-chief of Behavioral Neuroscience. It can be helpful to think of the project as a tentative title for the article it will become, a constant reminder of the scope it should span, recommends Bill Nazaroff, an environmental engineer at the University of California, Berkeley.
		\item Quote: Eileen White, associate director of the Cancer Institute of New Jersey in New Brunswick and a senior editor at Cancer Prevention Research, says that researchers should have a ``neat package'' of an interesting question, experiments to test it and a final answer. And a key to winning over editors and reviewers, says White, is having strong data to support conclusions. ``Some people don't appreciate the fact that a lot of weak data does not make up for having less, but more powerful, data,'' she says.
		\item Quote: Before starting to write the paper, authors should carefully choose a journal audience for their research story -- and initially aim for the highest-impact, highest-profile journal possible. ``The submission process is fast enough today; it's worth the effort of sending your paper to the highest journals where it belongs,'' says Hauber.
		\item It is better to have a smaller number of publications of outstanding quality than many decent publications, since recruitment of researchers and tenure-track faculty focuses on high-impact publications.
		\item ``Junior scientists should be thinking about the one paper that they'll place proudly at the top of their CV, job applications and grant proposals.'' -- Mark Hauber
		\item Quote: When should the writing begin? Research presented at a meeting or as a poster is almost certainly far enough along to begin writing up. But some researchers say that it is never too early to start -- students should be writing a little bit every day. Keeping a folder of pertinent literature and beginning with a simple outline of relevant points gleaned from that literature can provide the essential elements of a paper's introduction. Likewise, taking time during field or bench work to write short chunks of the materials and methods used can help to document these activities while they are fresh.
		\item Writers' tips: The key to effective writing: \vspace{-0.1cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -1pt
			\item You are only as good as your last paper -- previous success does not guarantee future acceptance.
			\item You've got to hook the editor with the abstract.
			\item Don't delete those files. Keep every version. You never know what aspect you can use for some other piece of writing.
			\item Writing is an amazingly long learning curve. Many authors say that they're still getting better as a writer after several decades.
			\item The most significant work is improved by subtraction. Keeping the clutter away allows a central message to be communicated with a broader impact.
			\item Write every day if possible.
			\item Once you've written what you wanted to convey, end it there. K.P.
			\end{enumerate}
		\item Look at papers from the same journal to determine their length and organization. Gauge the length of the paper and sections by the number of paragraphs. Determine the number of figures and references in the papers. Break up the ``daunting sections'' ``into more manageable portions''.
		\item To overcome writer's block, start by carrying out the easier tasks of writing first. E.g., contacting a collaborator, ``checking a reference,'' or edit material that was written the previous day.
		\item ``Recognizing that writing is a long process is valuable. Find a mentor in that process, somebody to guide and coach you.'' -- Bill Nazaroff
		\item Be aware of my working patterns, and write when I am most productive. Get myself into the writing zone, and get stuff done.
		\item ``Be clear and concise and use simple language whenever possible.''
		\item ``Important but poorly written papers could end up being sent back unreviewed by busy editors.''
		\item ``Editors stress the importance of clarity above all else, to help convey arguments and logic to them and to readers. They say that most writers make the mistake of assuming too much knowledge on the part of their audience. In reality, even at the most specialized journals, only a handful of readers will be such close colleagues that they don't need any contextual set-up.''
		\item Quote: Editors say that one way to identify holes or gaps in logic that would be vulnerable in peer review is to imagine a skeptical audience reading the manuscript. ``Think of the most adversarial reader you can imagine, and write to substantiate the veracity of your arguments and to anticipate criticisms and answer them,'' says Wojtal. Some editors suggest that `winning over' a skeptical editor, reader or reviewer should be the ultimate goal of any paper's abstract. ``Editors read the abstract and start formulating a thumbs-up or thumbs-down, looking for reasons to rip it apart,'' says White. ``You want them to form a positive point of view from the very beginning,'' she says. Leslie Sage, an astronomy editor at Nature, says authors should avoid an abstract structure that says: we did X, which told us Y, and has implications for Z. Instead, he says, start with why a reader should care about learning more about Z and then explain how this work furthers that goal.
		\item Quote: Likewise, says Nazaroff, the introduction should persuade readers ``that you know what you are talking about and have something new to teach them.'' Wojtal also advises authors to clearly distinguish between data in the results section and inferences about what they mean in the discussion section. This way, even if an editor or reviewer does not agree with a lab's interpretation of the work, he or she may still see the need to publish such an important data set.
		\item Quote: Often, less is more for junior scientists crafting manuscripts. The introduction need not cite every background article gathered, the results section should not archive every piece of data ever collected, and the discussion is not a treatise on the paper's subject. The writer must be selective, choosing only the references, data points and arguments that bolster the particular question at hand.
		\item ``Even polished authors go through an average of 10-12 drafts, and sometimes as many as 30.''
		\item Quote: Writers should ask not only the principal investigator to view drafts, but also every co-author, as well as fellow students or postdocs, and colleagues outside the immediate field of research. Lead authors should give co-authors set deadlines of 10 days to two weeks to suggest changes. Experienced authors counsel letting the draft sit for a few days before reading it with fresh eyes to catch mistakes or problems in flow. Blumberg prefers to read drafts aloud with his students to spot errors.
		\item Quote: When the paper is ready to submit, says Wojtal, the author should devise a cover letter that includes a brief synopsis of the article's argument, and suggestions for a few potential reviewers, as well as those who should be excluded. Such information, he says, can be very helpful to busy editors, who want to know who is familiar with the work and will be easy to reach. Authors should not suggest reviewers who are personal friends or institutional colleagues; including those people could immediately erode the editor's trust. Authors need to find a balance -- it is fine to exclude a couple of reviewers who are direct competitors or known naysayers, but restricting too many qualified reviewers can backfire. ``As an author, your job is to make the editor's job as easy as possible,'' says Blumberg.
		\item Address each comment of the reviewers step-by-step. Work through the lists of comments by the reviewers, ``and explain how the criticisms were addressed, or why they were not, in the resubmission cover letter to the editor.''
		\item ``A clear, succinct resubmission letter may result in an editor making their own decision rather than sending the paper back out for another round of review.''
		\item ``The worst thing an author can do is to ignore a reviewer's criticism and send it back without an explanation.'' -- Steven Wojtal
		\item ``If you strongly believe the reviewers have erred and that the editor should hear from you, definitely send an e-mail ... Be as polite as possible, stick to the facts, and keep it to the point.'' -- Mark Blumberg
		\item ``Young scientists would be wise to embrace written communication as the foundation of an academic career and the key to earning tenure, winning funding and, ultimately, sustaining a research program ... If your result is not published, you haven't done anything ... You might not set out to be an enthusiastic writer, but you should try to learn to love it.'' -- Mark Hauber
		\end{itemize}
	\item David A. Mackey, ``Recruiters \& Academia: Training peer reviewers,'' in {\it Nature}, Vol. 443, No. 7113, pp. 880, October 19, 2006. Available online at: \url{http://dx.doi.org/10.1038/nj7113-880b}; last accessed on January 1, 2010.
	\item Marwan Azar, ``Prospects: The method and madness of publishing,'' in {\it Nature}, Vol. 464, No. 7289, pp. 799, April 1, 2010. Available online at: \url{http://dx.doi.org/10.1038/nj7289-799b}; last accessed on January 1, 2010. \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item Quote: The process of publishing a scientific paper is twofold. Most important, it involves the attainment, integration, assessment and correction of knowledge, a process more commonly known as the `scientific method'. But it also requires navigating a minefield of administrative and bureaucratic issues.
		\item ``But although conducting the science that goes into writing a paper is rewarding, publishing has, unfortunately, as much to do with exasperating administrative issues.''
		\item ``their findings can be distilled into a cogent, cohesive and engaging scientific article'' [ Distill my research findings into ``a cogent, cohesive, and engaging scientific article.'' ]
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{enumerate}
%\item {\it Sci-edit.com} (SciEdit) ``is a scientific and medical editing service for non-native English speakers''; this service is NOT free: \url{http://www.sci-edit.com/About.htm}
\end{itemize}




\vspace{0.5in}
Notes on writing research papers: \vspace{-0.3cm}
\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -4pt
\item Explicitly address the following questions in the introduction of the paper in a succinct manner: \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item What is the problem?
	\item Why is the problem important?
	\item What has been done so far on the problem?
	\item What is the contribution of the paper on the problem?
	\item Is the contribution original? Explain why.
	\item Is the contribution non-trivial? Explain why.
	\end{enumerate}
\end{enumerate}



%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\subsection{\hspace{0.1in} Giving a Research Presentation on an EDA Topic}
\label{sssec:givingaresearchpresentationonanedatopic}

Giving a research presentation on an EDA topic: \vspace{-0.3cm}
\begin{itemize} \itemsep -4pt
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%	Introduction phase of the presentation
\item {\bf Introduction phase of the presentation}
\item Before telling me what you did, tell me why I should care $[$about your research$]$.
\ \rule{6in}{.1pt}
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%	General advice for giving presentations
\item {\bf General advice for giving presentations}
\item A great speaker could make any topic seem interesting and accessible, and some of the talks I ended up enjoying the most were ones I thought I wouldnÕt even understand!
\item During a presentation on my research, answer the following question, ``How is the big picture divided into subproblems, and where do you fit in?'' Now that the big picture is clear, what are the specific subproblem challenges? Which part of which subproblem are you working on? Some of this will necessarily get into details that not everyone in the audience can follow. If your efforts succeed, what will you have demonstrated? Another way to ask this is: what is the ``research question'' (or questions) being addressed here? In other words, ten years from now, when the hardware, software, etc. have all changed and the computers of the day make today's computers look like Tinkertoys, what fundamental nugget of an idea will still be considered relevant and applicable? This is often very hard to identify, and it may be that your own piece of the project contributes only a small part toward forming that Big Idea...
\item When giving a presentation on my research, take time to explain the motivation of the problem well, and be sure everyone understands (at least at a high level) why it's useful, interesting, etc.  This is time well spent: if people don't understand the ultimate goal, they probably won't pay attention to what you did.
\ \rule{6in}{.1pt}
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%	Advice for the Q\&A part of the presentation
\item {\bf Advice for the Q\&A part of the presentation}
\item ``Know how to listen, and you will profit even from those who talk badly.'' -- Plutarch, on Terry Tao's blog; see \url{http://mathgradblog.williams.edu/?p=17#more-17}
\item Valerie M. Balester, ``The Perfect Defense: The Oral Defense of a Dissertation,'' in {\it YouTube: Texas A\&M University, AggieMedia}, Google Inc., Mountain View, CA, October 26, 2010. Available online at: \url{http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=edQv9OKvfdU}; last accessed on August 9, 2013. [``Dr. Valerie Balester of Texas A\&M University talks about how to prepare and what to expect when defending your dissertation.''] \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item Ask the question to them rhetorically, to slow things down or when I don't know what are they asking about. This gives me sometime to think about the question, and how to answer it.
	\item Correct myself if I need to
	\item I need a little time to answer that. Can I just have a little moment to gather my thoughts?
	\item Take an attempt to answer; try to answer the question\dots
	\item Thank you for that question. That's a really good point.
	\item They are trying to see if I can think. Verbalize my thought process.
	\item When they ask you to make a revision to your dissertation, ask them to summarize the major points of the revision so that I can take notes about the major revision now.
	\item When you don't know, $[$you can say$]$: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item You asked me (repeat the question), and I'm not sure, but I think\dots
		\item If I knew {\it x}, I would say {\it y}.
		\item I don't know, but that the question has interesting implications, for example, if I knew that I could\dots
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{enumerate}
\end{itemize}
