% compiler design, construction, and optimization
\item {\bf compiler design, construction, and optimization}
\item Looking for self-motivated, team oriented software engineer to lead and sustain development effort for compiler environment to support an advanced programmable hardware engine
\item Responsible for design and implementation of compilers for low level and high level languages targeting a proprietary reconfigurable medium grained parallel processor
\item Must be enthusiastic self-starter interested in solving unusual problems and inventing syntactic constructs and back-end implementations in support of novel hardware features
\item You will work as an integral part of a small team to develop and deliver new compiler capabilities that span both the front-end and back-end of the compiler.
\item Participate in the entire development lifecycle of the compiler including specification, architecture, design, implementation, unit test and verification.
\item 10+ years experience with compiler front ends and  back ends, including parsing, code generation,  relocation fix-ups, etc.
\item Strong understanding of compiler theory and practice, including tools such as Lex and Yacc.
\item Experience implementing in multiple high level and assembly languages, and  willingness/ability to rapidly learn and use new implementation languages.
\item Ability to read and understand hardware architecture documentation and produce supporting compiler technology.
\item Must thoroughly understand computer architecture; parallel architecture background a plus.
\item Willingness to implement and maintain unit and regression tests. 
\item Experience with paradigmatic languages such as Lisp, Ruby, Python, Smalltalk, Haskell, etc. a plus.
\item Experience with parallel processors, reconfigurable processing, dataflow processing, and/or digital hardware design a large plus. 
\item Reproduce and resolve issues reported by internal and external application developers.
\item Create benchmarks and other tools used to measure and increase compiler performance and efficiency.
\item Help create and maintain automated regression tests for quick validation of compiler functionality.
\item Work with other team members to ensure that the compiler is ready for shipment.
\item Minimally requires BSEE/CS combined with 5-10+ yrs related 
\\ \rule{6in}{.1pt}
% concurrent programming, parallel computing (computer architecture), and compiler design \& construction (dynamic compilation and runtime profiling techniques)
\item {\bf concurrent programming, parallel computing (computer architecture), and compiler design \& construction (dynamic compilation and runtime profiling techniques)}
\item pursue research in programming models and compilation techniques for parallel programs
\item develop runtimes for parallel program optimization
\item architecture design to support efficient execution of parallel codes
\item We are seeking candidates with experience in both compiler optimization techniques, runtime systems and good architecture understanding to participate in research on managed runtimes and dynamic compilation. The ideal candidate will be able to design and implement hardware structures to aid dynamic compilation, runtime profiling techniques to monitor application behavior and trigger optimization, and design and implement dynamic compiler techniques that take advantage of runtime information to optimize the execution of parallel programs.
\\ \rule{6in}{.1pt}
% parallel computing, concurrent programming, performance analysis / optimization, and GPU architecture
\item {\bf parallel computing, concurrent programming, performance analysis / optimization, and GPU architecture}
\item evaluating and optimizing the performance of common concurrency patterns on graphics processing units (GPUs)
\item identifying the concurrency characteristics of general-purpose computing on GPUs (GPGPU)
\item develop techniques to identify the concurrency characteristics of general-purpose tasks off-loaded to GPUs
\item design and adapt synchronization mechanisms that leverage the architectural characteristics of GPUs
\item have experience in programming GPUs, concurrent programming, experimental evaluation, and familiarity with processor and memory hierarchy architectures and their impact on application and system performance
\item At least 2 years experience in Concurrent Programming
\item At least 2 years experience in Computer Architecture
\item At least 2 years experience in GPU Architecture 
\\ \rule{6in}{.1pt}
% performance analysis, \& thermal and power management
\item {\bf performance analysis, \& thermal and power management}
\item At least 4 years experience in computer science and performance analysis with a focus on system level performance and cache hierarchies in CMP systems
\item At least 1 year experience in power and thermal issues in CMP systems
\\ \rule{6in}{.1pt}
% Digital VLSI design
\item {\bf Digital VLSI design}
\item At least 2 years experience in circuit design, architecture and microarchitecture
\item At least 2 years experience in computer aided design
\\ \rule{6in}{.1pt}
% Digital (and Analog) VLSI design
\item {\bf Digital (and Analog) VLSI design}
\item At least 1 year experience in Circuit Design
\item At least 1 year experience in Simulation analysis
\item At least 1 year experience in CAD tool experience 
\item develop high-performance, energy-efficient VLSI circuits for application to advanced CMOS technologies
\item our projects include arrays, sensors, memory organization, power regulation \& distribution, and advanced 3D technology research
\\ \rule{6in}{.1pt}
% Analog/RF VLSI design
\item {\bf Analog/RF VLSI design}
\item At least 2 years experience in High Speed Circuit Design, especially I/Os and/or PLLs
\item At least 1 year experience in Custom Digital Design
\item At least 1 year experience in Circuit Design Tools/Languages such as Cadence, VHDLnet or comparable),
\item Basic knowledge in high-frequency laboratory measurement experience
\item Basic knowledge in behavioral modeling, simulation/analysis: Matlab 
\\ \rule{6in}{.1pt}
% embedded software development
\item {\bf embedded software development}
\item At least 2 years experience in cell programming (develop, implement, and optimize algorithms on the cell processor)
\item Parallel programming skills preferred
\\ \rule{6in}{.1pt}
% SDR (Software Defined Radio) Application Engineer
\item {\bf SDR (Software Defined Radio) Application Engineer}
\item Among other requirements: OFDMA or CDMA background required; and experience with SDR algorithms (Rake, FEC, Viterbi, Turbo Codec, Symbol processing)
\end{itemize}




%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\subsection{\hspace{0.1in} Innovations in EDA Research}
\label{innovationsinedaresearch}

Innovations in EDA research: \vspace{-0.3cm}
\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -4pt
\item engage in breakthrough, disruptive, or orthogonal innovation, rather than incremental innovation
\item be an expert in 1 (or 2) field(s), and be able to communicate across disciplines
\item be a connector who can synthesize ideas from different disciplines
\item do not make too rigid technological assumptions that limit generalizations. It is however important to understand the details ... {\bf The devil is in the details unfortunately}
\item do not make too rigid assumptions to get a broad perspective on the problems that may limit innovation.
\item do not spend undue time in securing industrial uptake of academic research... That is, do not worry about whether if the industry is going to adopt your innovations, algorithms, tools, and methodologies. Pursue good research, and the industry will adapt good technological innovations.
\item be innovative without challenging sound scientific practice or limiting industrial impact
\item determine what the value proposition of your product would be early on -- I need to know this early to earn angel or VC funding
\item create a new market segment with my R\&D effort/investment, rather than join a market segment that is packed/filled with big market players
\item pursue organic growth and development
\item attempt to come up with radical innovations that will generate a positive cash flow soon after implementation
\item be aware of my environment and the business ecosystem that I am in, such as being aware of market consolidation and production consolidation, and things that are getting commoditized
\item {\bf develop a sound plan for ``technology-to-product transition''}: \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item In business and engineering, new product development (NPD) is the term used to describe the complete process of bringing a new product or service to market. There are two parallel paths involved in the NPD process: one involves the idea generation, product design, and detail engineering; the other involves market research and marketing analysis. 
	\item {\bf continually improve research-to-product transition; start from doing good research to develop radically innovative technologies, and turn such technologies into products}
	\item {\bf having a good research-to-product development flow is an important factor in the success of a start-up}
	\item ``EDA 3.0: So you are an EDA startup?'' By Tom Kozas and Michael Sanie (03/17/2009 11:37 AM EDT), EDA DesignLine; see \url{http://www.edadesignline.com/howto/215900624;jsessionid=Q3ZN3TWP2APXKQSNDLRSKH0CJUNN2JVN?pgno=1} and \url{http://www.edadesignline.com/215900624;jsessionid=Q3ZN3TWP2APXKQSNDLRSKH0CJUNN2JVN?printableArticle=true} 
	\item ``EDA 3.0: EDA startup guidelines'' By Nicolas Mokhoff (03/17/2009 9:26 AM EDT), {\it EE Times}; see \url{http://www.eetimes.com/showArticle.jhtml?articleID=215900871}
	\end{enumerate}
\item HPÕs Rules of the Garage (Hewlett Packard): \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item Believe you can change the world.
	\item Work quickly, keep the tools unlocked, work whenever.
	\item Know when to work alone and when to work together.
	\item Share -- tools, ideas. Trust your colleagues.
	\item No politics. No bureaucracy. (These are ridiculous in a garage.)
	\item The customer defines a job well done.
	\item Radical ideas are not bad ideas.
	\item Invent different ways of working. 
	\item Make a contribution every day. If it doesn't contribute, it doesn't leave the garage.
	\item Believe that together we can do anything.
	\item Invent.
	\end{enumerate}
\item David Packard's Eleven Simple Rules: \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item Think first of the other fellow. 
	\item Build up the other personÕs sense of importance. 
	\item Respect the other personÕs personality rights. 
	\item Give sincere appreciation.  
	\item Eliminate the negative.  
	\item Avoid openly trying to reform people.
	\item Try to understand the other person.
	\item Check first impressions. 
	\item Take care with the little details.
	\item Develop genuine interest in people. 
	\item Keep it up.
	\end{enumerate}
\item the revised HP way by HP's former CEO Carly Fiorina: \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item Trust Your people
	\item Match the right people to the right job
	\item Hire the best
	\item Continually learn
	\item Motivate by vision
	\item Manage through processes
	\end{enumerate}
\item The HP Way, \url{http://www.hpalumni.org/hp_way.htm}: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item We have trust and respect for individuals. \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item We approach each situation with the belief that people want to do a good job and will do so, given the proper tools and support.
		\item We attract highly capable, diverse, innovative people and recognize their efforts and contributions to the company.
		\item HP people contribute enthusiastically and share in the success that they make possible. 
		\end{enumerate}
	\item We focus on a high level of achievement and contribution. \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item Our customers expect HP products and services to be of the highest quality and to provide lasting value.
		\item To achieve this, all HP people, especially managers, must be leaders who generate enthusiasm and respond with extra effort to meet customer needs.
		\item Techniques and management practices which are effective today may be outdated in the future.
		\item For us to remain at the forefront in all our activities, people should always be looking for new and better ways to do their work. 
		\end{enumerate}
	\item We conduct our business with uncompromising integrity. \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item Our customers expect HP products and services to be of the highest quality and to provide lasting value.
		\item To achieve this, all HP people, especially managers, must be leaders who generate enthusiasm and respond with extra effort to meet customer needs.
		\item Techniques and management practices which are effective today may be outdated in the future.
		\item For us to remain at the forefront in all our activities, people should always be looking for new and better ways to do their work. 
		\end{enumerate}
	\item We achieve our common objectives through teamwork. \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item We recognize that it is only through effective cooperation within and among organizations that we can achieve our goals.
		\item Our commitment is to work as a worldwide team to fulfill the expectations of our customers, shareholders and others who depend upon us.
		\item The benefits and obligations of doing business are shared among all HP people.
		\end{enumerate}
	\item We encourage flexibility and innovation. \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item We create an inclusive work environment which supports the diversity of our people and stimulates innovation.
		\item We strive for overall objectives which are clearly stated and agreed upon, and allow people flexibility in working toward goals in ways that they help determine are best for the organization.
		\item HP people should personally accept responsibility and be encouraged to upgrade their skills and capabilities through ongoing training and development.
		\item This is especially important in a technical business where the rate of progress is rapid and where people are expected to adapt to change.
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{enumerate}
\item Simula Culture: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item Reference: Simula Research Laboratory, ``Simula Culture,'' Simula Research Laboratory, Fornebu, Norway. Available online at: \url{http://simula.no/about/culture} and \url{http://ploneint.simula.no/pdf/simula_culture/simula_culture.pdf}; last accessed on July 28, 2012.
	\item Reference: Simula Research Laboratory, ``The Simula Code of Ethics,'' Fornebu, Norway. Available online at: \url{http://simula.no/about/the-simula-code-of-ethics}; last accessed on July 28, 2012.
	\end{enumerate}
\item evolutionary incremental innovation: \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -3pt
	\item low uncertainty
	\item linear and continuous trajectory
	\item short-term
	\item exploitation of existing technology
	\item idea generation (project charter) $\Rightarrow$ concept feasibility (business plan) $\Rightarrow$ capability development (launch proposal) $\Rightarrow$ ramp up (sanity check) $\Rightarrow$ launch
	\item weaknesses: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item low gatekeeper knowledge (lack of expertise knowledge about product domain/technology) leads to poor judgments
		\item slow and serial
		\item concept frozen too early
		\item focused on milestones and deliverables, not the customer(s)
		\item long review preparation time
		\item narrow criteria $[$for success$]$
		\item maturity focus versus learning focus ... $[$employees don't grow personally and professionally; they lose touch with contemporary market trends, and their skills and knowledge become out of date$]$
		\end{enumerate} 
	\end{enumerate}
\item revolutionary radical innovation: \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -3pt
	\item high uncertainty in terms of technical challenges, market and organizational risks, and resource utilization/requirement
	\item sporadic and discontinuous trajectory
	\item business model and plan evolves through discovery-based learning
	\item long-term
	\item sporadic - starts and stops, \& dead ends and revivals
	\item nonlinear - detours, \& recycling back through activities in response to discontinuities and setbacks
	\item stochastic - waxing and waning of interest and funding, key players come and go, \& priorities change
	\item context dependent - corporate culture, history, personalities, informal relations, and experience all create a mix of accelerating and retarding factors
	\item formality of process can increase gradually over time as uncertainty is reduced with gradual progress (accomplished milestones) made in the projects
	\item freeze the concept later in the project than with incremental innovation: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item longer concept development time
		\item shorter implementation time
		\item later part of concept development time overlaps with early phase implementation time; this can help avoid missing newly emerging technologies
		\end{enumerate}
	\item is effective for changing business environment
	\item need for strong creative chaos and strong guiding structure
	\item fuzzy front end
	\item research $\Rightarrow$ technology $\Rightarrow$ design $\Rightarrow$ production $\Rightarrow$ market --- the first two phases involve radical innovation, and the last two involve incremental innovation (Kaizen)
	\item design is at the interface of radical innovation and incremental innovation
	\item exploration of new technology
	\item fuzzy front-end elements: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item Opportunity Identification
		\item Opportunity Analysis
		\item Idea Genesis
		\item Idea Selection
		\item Concept \& Technology Development
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{enumerate}
\item structured NPPD (New Product \& Process Development) strategy
\item Avant-garde means ``advance guard'' or ``vanguard''. The adjective form is used in English, to refer to people or works that are experimental or innovative, particularly with respect to art, culture, and politics.
\item the market was the source of new ideas for directing R\&D, which had a reactive role in the process. The stages of the ``market pull'' model are: Market need $\Rightarrow$ Development $\Rightarrow$ Manufacturing $\Rightarrow$ Sales
\item types of research: \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item exploratory research: structures and identifies new problems
	\item constructive research: develops solutions to a problem
	\item empirical research: tests the feasibility of a solution using empirical evidence
	\end{enumerate}
\item diffusion of innovation is a theory of how, why, and at what rate new ideas and technology spread through cultures: \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item theory on the ``diffusion of innovation''
	\item the diffusion of innovation theory
	\item technology adoption lifecycle ... $[$there is a nice graph in Wikipedia to denote the proportion of innovators, early adopters, early majority, late majority, and laggards in a population$]$
	\item technology acceptance model: perceived usefulness and perceived ease-of-use
	\end{enumerate}
\item From a layman's perspective, the technological maturity can be broken down into five distinct stages: \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item Bleeding edge: any technology that shows high potential but hasn't demonstrated its value or settled down into any kind of consensus ... Early adopters may win big, or may be stuck with a white elephant.
	\item Leading edge: a technology that has proven itself in the marketplace but is still new enough that it may be difficult to find knowledgeable personnel to implement or support it.
	\item State of the art: when everyone agrees that a particular technology is the right solution.
	\item Dated: still useful, still sometimes implemented, but a replacement leading edge technology is readily available.
	\item Obsolete: has been superseded by state-of-the-art technology, maintained but no longer implemented.
	\end{enumerate}
\item take note of uptrends and downtrends in industry
\item emerging technologies is a general term used to denote significant technological developments that in effect, broach new territory in some significant way in their field. Examples of currently emerging technologies include nanotechnology, biotechnology, cognitive science, robotics, and artificial intelligence
\item Emerging technologies are those which represent new and significant developments within a field; converging techologies represent previously distinct fields which are in some way moving towards stronger inter-connection and similar goals.
\item A disruptive technology or disruptive innovation is marketing speak for a technological innovation that improves a product or service in ways that the market does not expect, typically by being lower priced or designed for a different set of consumers.
\item What Does Value Proposition Mean? A business or marketing statement that summarizes why a consumer should buy a product or use a service. This statement should convince a potential consumer that one particular product or service will add more value or better solve a problem than other similar offerings.
\item Unique Selling Proposition (also Unique Selling Point)
\item proprietary technology, sustainable advantage, and follow-on products
\item ``I take notice of companies that are going to change the fundamental rules, NOT incrementally improve things. 20x runtime and 100x capacity is a rule breaker. Oasys is as interesting as it is compelling.'' -- Joe Costello, former CEO, Cadence Design Systems; see \url{http://www.oasys-ds.com/} ... last viewed May 27, 2009
\item develop and grow my portfolio of innovative formal verification technology patents
\item Develop EDA tools to empower engineers, not to replace them
\end{enumerate}




%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\subsection{\hspace{0.1in} Career Advice for Research in EDA}
\label{careeradviceforresearchineda}

Career advice for research in EDA: \vspace{-0.3cm}
\begin{itemize} \itemsep -4pt
\item Come up with ideas for a new product/service, develop design plans, prove the concept with a prototype/simulation, pitch it to your managers and senior management (corporate executives/C-Suite); that is, ``get an opportunity to have an idea floated by someone, and try to get funding. That's quite an experience, very broadening'' -- Rico Mariani ... knowing your facts, knowing what's important, knowing what's good for the business, what makes a good business case ... Think of Bill Mullen's (VP @ Synopsys in 2007) experience in getting funding for his power and timing analysis/optimization project; he had developed the prototype, and had three/four members in the team then
\item Prof. Ian Parberry's philosophy for running the Game Development Research Group (GDRG) at University of North Texas (UNT) is given as follows; see \url{http://www.eng.unt.edu/ian/research/philosophy.html}; available May 8, 2010. GDRG values research that is: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item Deep, not superficial. GDRG papers will not be hastily-written, shallow, nor destined to obscurity.
	\item Done in small groups, not large anonymous ones. We will be sharing knowledge and understanding with graduate and undergraduate students, not working in large groups where it is hard to assign credit, nor working in isolation.
	\item Tied into the ``web of research,'' not done in a vacuum. We will acknowledge prior related work in full, aiming for a paper that is significant enough to be cited in turn.
	\item Innovative, not repetitive. We will follow new threads, not embroider past achievements.
	\item Well-grounded, not half-baked. We will implement systems, not just describe them. Experimental data generated by these systems will be large enough to achieve statistical significance.
	\item Knowledge, not just data. 
	\end{enumerate}
\item move people's focus/attention from minor problems, which you can solve, to major problems that you are seeking help with or opinions for
\item ask penetrating questions about the product/technology
\item don't be a ``one hit wonder''; the term ``One Hit Wonder'' describes an artist or band who had great popularity with a single song, and who then never reached such popularity again
\item beware of regularly developing one-trick ponies that may cause inconvenience about people; the slang term ``one trick pony'' is used to refer to something that can only be used for one very specific application
\item make a plan for what you do
\item be deliberate about what you do
\item be autonomous, and drive technology
\item {\bf ask, ``How bad can it be?''} ... When you make a mistake, always ask this question. At the worst, move on!
\item have fun with what you do, experiment with your product to see how well it works and determine its failure modes, keep up with current and emerging technologies (even if it is outside of your field), and be creative (try new things to make your product/innovation better)
\item nurture your ideas, and realize them ... ideas are worthless
\item suppose it $[$this job$]$ doesn't work out, you go get another job! -- Jim Gray to Catharine van Ingen
\item when/if you are not having fun, what's the point? -- Catharine van Ingen
\item Edsger Dijkstra's tips for PhD students: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{itemize} \itemsep -4pt
	\item Never compete with colleagues
	\item Try the most difficult thing you can do
	\item Choose what is scientifically healthy and relevant. Don't compromise on scientific integrity. 
	\end{itemize}
\item choose work that is challenging and interesting, because it will keep your attention and help you learn ... also, it saves some time playing with good ideas
\item read several sources for a concept (thoroughly), so that I can see different approaches to the material and then synthesize what's most relevant
\item if I cannot obtain a permanent full-time position at an organization I would like to work for, I can take a temporary job or paid internship there, or volunteer there as an unpaid intern
\item when I take up a job, think about acquiring the skills that I will need for the next position that I am aiming for
\item take relevant classes for research, such as research methodology, statistics, technical writing, and management
\item get the $[$inside$]$ scoop, and learn about a prospective department's tenure practices by talking to faculty and students
\item become aware of trends that may affect hiring, such as recession, the emergence of new subfields, and rates of faculty retirement
\item get teaching experience in graduate school, or as a postdoc, before I apply
\item {\bf comparisons of tools on a set of benchmarks cannot yield infinite speedup; all speedups should be indicated as a finite number, say 10x or 50x}
\item do my salary homework (e.g., see IEEE-USA salary survey), but be reasonable
\item use my job to my advantage... If my first academic job is less than desirable, do the best job that I can, author or co-author a solid piece, send out applications, and get out of there so I can get on the tenure track
\item do more of the heavy lifting, or legwork, in research projects
\item never let the best (is it really the best?) get in the way of better: while building the best technology, but taking some steps pragmatically forward is a much better thing for everybody... We learn by doing
\item 4 Ps in R\&D: papers, projects, programs, and people 
\item ``pick a good project, and pick a good problem (that's the most important thing... to invent hard stuff) in many areas and advancing the state of the art in those things''  -- Jim Gray, interview for Microsoft's ``Behind the Code'' series on the ResearchChannel
\item ``the goal is to find something that is simple enough to make progress on it'' -- Jim Gray, interview for Microsoft's ``Behind the Code'' series on the ResearchChannel
\item ``develop a strong mix of breath and depth'' -- Jim Gray, interview for Microsoft's ``Behind the Code'' series on the ResearchChannel
\item ``be able to go back to first principles when solving problems'' -- Jim Gray, interview for Microsoft's ``Behind the Code'' series on the ResearchChannel
\item ``say what you mean, and mean what you say'' -- Jim Gray, interview for Microsoft's ``Behind the Code'' series on the ResearchChannel
\item ``know the reasons for the things you do'' -- Jim Gray, interview for Microsoft's ``Behind the Code'' series on the ResearchChannel
\item avoid teams that are micromanaged by supervisors, managers, and/or advisors that act like control freaks; { \bf beware of bureaucratic people, ``heavy-handed legal department,'' micromanagement, narcism, and technophobia} ... Similarly, avoid technophobes.
\item avoid engaging in one-upmanship, and reduce/eliminate rankism; do not dish out putdowns ... Do not be bent on these, since they affect the unity and productivity of the team
\item develop a specialized skill that is not taught at most universities
\item become a shockingly good programmer
\item figure out how to help chip designers achieve wickedly high performance chips -- such as general-purpose processor, graphics processors, or DSP designs at 45 nm, 32 nm, and below
\item keep an ``open door policy,'' so that people feel welcomed to come in and talk to you
\item keep building my network of research collaborators (from undergraduate through PhD and beyond)
\item keep track of my research interests, including its history since my undergraduate days at the University of Adelaide
\item {\bf regularly check if my research topic is too broad/narrow}
\item research area/field/topic: \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -3pt
	\item area: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item extent, range, or scope
		\item field of study, or a branch of a field of study
		\end{enumerate}
	\item field: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item a sphere of activity, interest, etc., esp. within a particular business or profession
		\item an area of human activity or interest
		\item a topic, subject, or area of academic interest or specialization
		\item a branch of knowledge
		\end{enumerate}
	\item topic: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item a subject of conversation or discussion
		\item the subject or theme of a discourse or of one of its parts
		\item thesis, subject matter
		\item the subject of a speech, essay, thesis, or discourse
		\item a subject of discussion or conversation
		\item a subdivision of a theme, thesis, or outline
		\item the subject of any distinct portion of a discourse, or argument, or literary composition
		\item the general or main subject of the whole
		\item a subject, as of conversation or of thought
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{enumerate}
\item bring computational thinking to other fields
\item {\bf learn how industrial-scale EDA research is carried out, and do {\bf that} kind of research}
\item Thomas Friedman's commencement speech at Grinnell College on May 18, 2009: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item Do the synthesis and connect the dots from $[$my$]$ different interests ... the whole buffet in liberal arts: mastered 2 or more (quite) different fields, use the framework in one to think afresh about the other, and mash them together into something that no one else has thought of... do not spend $[$my$]$ whole life in one silo
	\item ``If it is not happening, it's because I'm not doing it''
	\end{enumerate}
\item from a guest blog by Prof. Andrew B. Kahng from UCSD: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item \url{http://www.edadesignline.com/guest_blogs/217200218}
	\item ``Does the EDA industry have a roadmap?'', in EDA DesignLine (04/27/2009 2:02 PM EDT)
	\item Roadmaps look ahead. For example, the 2001 ITRS told us that power and productivity imperatives would drive the industry to {\bf SOC design methodology convergence} and a {multicore-centric future}, and that {\bf embedded software} and the {\bf design-manufacturing interface} presented looming crises. {\bf The implications of such predictions cannot be addressed by crash programs instituted only after wakeup calls; they demand proactive and sustained attention.}
	\item reduced risk of another type of delivery error, namely, solutions that are plain wrong or mismatched to designer needs
	\item I can say that {\bf there are really only around 6000 EDA R\&D heads worldwide at any time.} This number is at best stable (cf. industry revenues, research funding levels, etc.).
	\item Some of these R\&D resources are inevitably spent on fruitless directions, and are also subject to overlapping efforts (competitors build equivalent technologies, and geography (North America, Asia-Pacific, EU) creates triple-redundancy in funding of research programs)
	\item As the 6000 number includes professors and graduate students, this is clearly not a large resource; it must be as well-targeted as possible.
	\item The wisdom at Intel and many other semiconductor companies is that {\bf around 6-7 years are needed to bring a new EDA technology ``from DAC paper to production flow''}. Again, correct directions must be identified as early as possible. 
	\item $[$the$]$ industry $[$needs to focus on its$]$ priorities, $[$and establish$]$ agreements on non-differentiating (``commodity'') infrastructure and standards, so that these crises can be tackled: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item software and concurrency
		\item analog/RF
		\item die-package-board
		\item die stacking
		\end{enumerate} 
	\item with a fixed number of EDA R\&D engineers, we need to stop working on some issues
	\item In the big picture, who wins a power format or current-source model or process variation model war is less critical to semiconductor industry health than consensus, interoperability, and moving on to fundamental design technology challenges.
	\item EDA roadmaps: \vspace{-0.3cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item International Technology Roadmap for Semiconductors (ITRS): \vspace{-0.1cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item the main EDA roadmap is found in the {\bf Design chapter of the ITRS}, which provides quantified technology needs and potential solutions across system-level, logic- and physical-level, verification, DFT and DFM design technology arenas.
			\item The closely aligned {\bf ITRS System Drivers chapter} roadmaps key product drivers and aligns with the iNEMI (International Electronics Manufacturing Initiative) roadmap
			\end{enumerate}
		\item MEDEA+ EDA roadmap; see \url{http://www.medeaplus.org/index.php}
		\item Japan's STRJ Working Group 1's roadmap for design technology
		\end{enumerate}
	\item $[$build up and align my research portfolio with EDA roadmaps$]$
	\item Questions to ask concerning roadmaps: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item EDA engineers $[$and grad students$]$: ``Do you know your company's $[$or research lab's$]$ roadmap?''
		\item EDA managers $[$and professors$]$: ``How much of your product roadmap and R\&D budget is directed to solving long-term -- i.e., more than 7 years -- design technology needs?''
		\item EDA customers: ``Do you proactively define (at what time horizon), or do you just accept and live with, the roadmaps of your EDA suppliers?''
		\end{enumerate}
	\item Arguably, design technologists today do not have a roadmap -- at least, not in the way that manufacturing technologists do.
	\item Many companies are in the throes of reinventing themselves; nearly all are focused on a 1-quarter horizon and not a 20-quarter horizon. Perhaps those MRMs (management review meetings) with key customers and partners (Intel, TSMC, etc.) are a sufficient roadmapping process for EDA. 
	\item \url{http://www.edadesignline.com/guest_blogs/} or \url{http://www.edadesignline.com/guest_blogs/217500437}
	\item ``Risk-reward structures in EDA,'' in EDA DesignLine, May 15, 2009
	\item {\bf The EDA Innovator's Dilemma} -- In the ``innovator's dilemma'' (Christensen, 1997), a market leader competes by adding incremental features to its market-leading product until eventually displaced by a ``disruptive technology'' in one of two ways. : \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item In low-end disruption, the market leader's stream of added features eventually overshoots the market need, opening the door to simpler and lower-cost solutions. The history of Tangent, Avant!, Magma, Silicon Perspective, (Sierra, Atoptech, etc.) in the SP\&R space is a good example of cyclical low-end disruption in EDA... It recalls an old March 2003 quote, ``SOC designers ultimately can't use big Swiss Army knives stuffed with too many back-end EDA technology options,'' and the saying that ``those who don't learn from history are doomed to repeat it''.
		\item In new-market disruption, a disruptive technology opens up markets that did not previously exist. From the analyst and investor standpoint, a key challenge for the EDA industry is that it doesn't experience nearly enough new-market disruption. 
		\end{enumerate}
	\item {\bf The EDA Innovator's Solution}: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item The ``innovator's solution'' (Christensen and Raynor, 2003) is a set of precepts and strategies with respect to market segmentation, leadership, growth, etc., according to which successful companies can identify, develop and unleash the next disruptive technology.
		\item Two key elements of the ``innovator's solution'' are: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item outsourcing of commoditized, ``more than good enough'' solutions
			\item strategic investment in disruption-based growth
			\end{enumerate}
		\item Unfortunately, the EDA industry's outsourcing and investment doesn't match the ``innovator's solution'' model: companies still compete on commodities (back-end data models, power formats, etc.) while outsourcing long-range R\&D
		\item {\bf Why does this fail as an ``innovator's solution''?}: \vspace{-0.2cm}
			\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
			\item {\bf First, outsourcing of R\&D is neither sufficient nor stable.}
			\item The ``research funding gap'' mentioned in my last posting is a world-wide insufficiency that is, essentially, a ``tragedy of the commons''. This has occurred despite the efforts of many champions across companies, consortia, and funding agencies who have tried to maintain research investment levels and well-considered project portfolios.
			\item As for stability, the past few years have seen the pools of startup-based ``outsourced EDA R\&D'', as well as EDA research at universities and consortia, dry up as a consequence of macroeconomics and new funding targets (cleantech, biotech, homeland security, etc.).
			\item {\bf Second, long-term success is difficult when one has outsourced the strategic R\&D that gives rise to future disruptive technologies.} 
			\end{enumerate}
		\end{enumerate}
	\item Consider the risk-reward structure in an established EDA (or, IC design) company. Technology assets are primarily software (C++ or Verilog), the original developers are long gone, and there is a lot of ``legacy'' in the IP portfolio. This leads to a highly risk-averse profile, i.e., a tendency toward ``don't touch'' and ``leave well enough alone''.
	\item Legacy code -- despite the benefits of seasoning and reuse -- eventually becomes an albatross. Given the incremental product evolution that is inherent in the ``innovator's dilemma'' (recall the path to low-end disruption!), it is no wonder that market leaders can end up far off target with their ``next-generation'' products. 
	\item I would claim that low-end disruption in EDA does not resemble laser mask writers being displaced by e-beam mask writers, or vertically integrated steel mills being replaced by mini-mills. All too often, the periodic event in the EDA world is not the emergence of a new and disruptive technology, but simply a mundane, inertial loss of agility and ability to respond to the market.
	\item Next, consider the risk-reward structure for academic researchers. The first point to understand is that ``risk'' equals ``effort'' (how many researcher-months of salary and benefits and opportunity costs are sunk into a project), and ``reward'' equals ``likelihood that a respectable and impactful publication is the result''.
	\item The classic assumption is that research targets exist along a continuum. At one end, there is low-risk-low-reward research: incremental, turn-the-crank, with some ``minimum publishable unit'' outcome. At the other end, there is high-risk-high-reward research: e.g., trying to prove that P != NP. Depending on personality, talents, career stage, and other factors, academics choose their targets along this continuum. But in EDA research, again we've got it backward.
	\item For EDA researchers today, speculative, far-out research often has low risk but high reward. An example from my past: very few comparisons or proofs of superiority were needed to publish that first paper on physical design of quantum-dot cellular automata based chips.
	\item On the other hand, highly incremental research can require an enormous amount of effort and industry-grade experimental infrastructure to support any claimed contribution.
	\item Again drawing on examples from my past: making the next speed-quality improvement in KL-FM bipartitioning or mixed analytic and partitioning-based global placement are very high-risk research propositions. This anomaly is magnified when reviewer criteria set ``not novel'' as grounds for rejection.
	\item The result: academic researchers constantly pose ``new'' problem formulations (escaping the duty of apples-to-apples comparison with previous works), blurring the leading edge of knowledge and ultimately slowing progress on real problems.
	\item Question: How can EDA researchers regain a risk-reward tradeoff that correctly associates high reward and high impact with high risk?
	\item More than 10 years ago, the MARCO Gigascale Silicon Research Center established the Bookshelf of Fundamental CAD Algorithms (see ``Toward CAD-IP Reuse: A Web Bookshelf of Fundamental Algorithms'', A. E. Caldwell, A. B. Kahng and I. L. Markov, IEEE Design and Test, May-June 2002, and VLSI CAD Bookshelf 2 ). See \url{http://vlsicad.eecs.umich.edu/BK/}
	\item The idea of the Bookshelf was to provide a repository for reusable ``CAD-IP'' -- source codes, benchmark data, evaluation scripts, etc. -- to enable R\&D engineers in both academia and industry to advance the leading edge without having to reinvent the wheel. The food for thought in this posting: Might it be time to revisit some of the ideas that motivated the creation of the Bookshelf?
	\item An EDA company that better manages the ball and chain of legacy code will have greater agility and more resources that can be directed toward new markets and new technologies. This implies that companies must make greater investments in foundations and infrastructures for product and technology innovation. In the context of IC design and EDA, this includes software engineering methodology, software infrastructure, and fundamental algorithms (e.g., repurposable optimization engines, machine learning engines, etc.).
	\item In the EDA research context, the MARCO GSRC Bookshelf promoted open-source publication, along with standardized benchmarking and comparison methodologies. By reducing the overheads of research at the leading edge, such culture changes can effectively reduce the ``research funding gap'' and rebalance risk-reward structures toward research that has impact on core technology challenges.
	\item The past decade has also seen an important addition to the original Bookshelf concept: simplification. When researchers release elegant, compact codes that embody the leading edge in astonishingly few lines of code -- global routers from Michigan, or synthesis packages from Berkeley -- the impact across both academia and industry can be profound. If conference and journal reviewers and funding agencies follow suit, rewarding or incenting simplifications and productivity (speedup) improvements at least on par with 5 percent and ``new problem'' advances, then the world will have changed for the better. 
	\end{enumerate}
\item from a blog by Peggy Aycinena: \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item \url{http://www.edadesignline.com/guest_blogs/;jsessionid=AC2OUH2T42JMQQSNDLOSKHSCJUNN2JVN}
	\item ``Open source EDA software defeats Lock-in --- Dream on,'' in EDA DesignLine Engineering Guest Blog, June 10, 2009
	\item ``You have to distinguish between open source software and open source EDA software. There's a big difference. For open source software, you just need to create an application, but for open source EDA software you also need to have a scientific background, need to understand physical design, and have to have some mathematical algorithms to code. In the case of a synthesis tool, for instance, you have to be able to minimize the gate count while also improving the timing closure. Cadence or Synopsys spend a lot of money on research developing these types of features, a $[$level of resource$]$ completely lacking in the open source EDA community.''
	\item ``The EDA vendor community understands today that interoperability is very important, but this is frequently a weakness in the open source community. Open source EDA developers often try to invent their own file formats. Very few of them use industry standard formats to avoid having to start from scratch. Through FEL $[$Fedora Electronic Lab$]$, we are trying to change that, however, to convince developers to work so they can interchange their data easily. If we can share data between the different applications developed by the open source EDA community and the vendor tools, it will be a way to encourage tool users and designers to use and contribute to the open source tools.''
	\end{enumerate}
\item Takako Hashimoto, ``Seven Practices of Working Mother Researchers,'' IEEE Women in Engineering meeting/seminar. Available online at: \url{http://womenandmath.files.wordpress.com/2010/09/brochure100811takako.pdf}; last accessed on November 22, 2010. [ Also, see: Caroline Series, ``Seven Practices of Working Mother Researchers,'' in the {\it Women in Mathematics} blog, September 30, 2010. Available online at: \url{http://womenandmath.wordpress.com/2010/09/30/seven-practices-of-working-mother-researchers/}; last accessed on November 22, 2010. ]: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item Prof. Takako Hashimoto; IEEE JC WIE Vice Chair; Associate Professor, Chiba University of Commerce
	\item ``Consider Woman Life as an Optimization Problem: Take not a local maximum point but a real maximum having a long-term view''
	\item ``Community formation provides high added value''
	\item Seven Practices of Working Mother Researchers: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item Never give up your research work.
		\item Conduct good communication.
		\item Find and leverage your time.
		\item Show a visible result.
		\item Defend your home.
		\item Education and home discipline are of great value.
		\item Be active.
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{enumerate}
\item ``Analog adoption takes a lot of time. Any adoption of an analog tool probably takes 5-7 years, and this is beyond the time horizon of most VCs. To get money from a VC, you'd have to have an adoption time of 3-5 years. You'll never get there. In analog EDA, the slow road is the way to go.'' -- Henry Chang, in an interview with Richard Goering, in Expert's Corner: ``Analog complexity demands new verification approaches,'' By Richard Goering, 12/19/07; see \url{http://www.scdsource.com/experts.php?id=64&page=1}
\item Is this improvement incremental or substantial?
\item Am I doing fundamental research on $[$insert my research topic$]$?
\item Have I recently attended any ``eye-opening talks'' related to my research?
\item ``Users are exceptionally good at what they do, completely overloaded in their daily jobs, reluctant to transition away from a known good solution, and realistic about EDA marketing claims. Users donÕt do science projects. They very well understand the realities of bringing up a new tool, they have hard budgets and P\&Ls to deal with, they always have other fish to fry, and they have very conservative profiles when it comes to benefit-cost propositions.'' -- DFM luminary, Prof. Andrew Kahng, \url{http://www.scdsource.com/experts.php?id=178}
\end{itemize}



%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\subsection{\hspace{0.1in} Advice on Choosing an Advisor}
\label{sssec:advicechoosingadvisor}

Advice on choosing an advisor: \vspace{-0.3cm}
\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -4pt
\item Check if the professor/researcher has the following characteristics/attributes: \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item {\bf Listen patiently}. Give the student time to get to issues they find sensitive or embarrassing.
	\item {\bf Build a relationship}. Simple joint activities -- walks across campus, informal conversations over coffee, attending a lecture together -- will help to develop rapport. Take cues from the student as to how close they wish this relationship to be.
	\item {\bf Don't abuse your authority}. Don't ask students to do personal work, such as mowing lawns, baby-sitting, and typing.
	\item {\bf Nurture self-sufficiency}. Your goal is not to ``clone'' yourself, but to encourage confidence and independent thinking.
	\item {\bf Establish ``protected time'' together}. Try to minimize interruptions by telephone calls or visitors.
	\item {\bf Share yourself}. Invite students to see what you do, both on and off the job. Tell of your own successes and failures. Let the student see your human side and encourage the student to reciprocate.
	\item {\bf Provide introductions}. Help the student develop a professional network and build a community of mentors.
	\item {\bf Be constructive}. Critical feedback is essential to spur improvement, but do it kindly and temper criticism with praise when deserved.
	\item {\bf Don't be overbearing}. Avoid dictating choices or controlling a student's behavior.
	\item {\bf Find your own mentors}. New advisers, like new students, benefit from guidance by those with more experience.
	\item Reference: Empowering Leadership Alliance, ``Advice for New Mentors,'' Empowering Leadership Alliance, Rice University, Houston, TX, 2010. Available online at: \url{http://empoweringleadership.rice.edu/Content.aspx?id=194}; last accessed on June 11, 2012. Also, see American Chemical Society's {\it Project Seed: Student, Mentor and Coordinator Handbook}
	\end{enumerate}
\item A. Lee, C. Dennis, and P. Campbell. Nature's guide for mentors. Nature, 447(7146):791--797, June 14, 2007 \cite{Lee2007}.
\end{enumerate}



%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\subsection{\hspace{0.1in} Advice on Picking a Ph.D. Dissertation Topic}
\label{adviceonpickingathesistopic}

Advice on picking a Ph.D. dissertation topic: \vspace{-0.3cm}
\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -4pt
\item Choose a topic you love: \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item This may be the most important criteria
	\item You're going to be spending so much time with this project, and your quality of life will be much better if these hours are spent enjoyably
	\item What's more, the quality of your research, writing, and arguments will be much better if you feel genuine passion for your work
	\item Choose a topic you find both fascinating, and socially $[$intellectually$]$ significant $[$Or, one that may lead to the commercialization of your research as a spin-off or start-up$]$
	\item Never let someone pressure you into writing about a certain topic!
	\end{enumerate}
\item Pick something your advisor finds interesting and is knowledgeable about: \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item Of course, if this is not possible, you might want to change your advisor instead of changing your topic
	\end{enumerate}
\item Pick a topic that will be helpful in your career path: \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item If your goal is an academic career, pick a topic that you can easily modify into journal articles or a book, and that will lend itself well to future research
	\item If you want to work at a teaching oriented institution, consider a topic you can use in the classroom
	\item If you are going into industry, choose a topic that will make you more marketable.
	\end{enumerate}
\item Find a topic that establishes your niche in your field: \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item Do your research and find a topic that fits into existing bodies of literature, but that builds upon theory and expands it $[$It is easier to do this, rather than to create a new field (of your own)$]$
	\end{enumerate}
\item Choose research that is unique: \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item Do significant research to make sure this topic has not been done before
	\item Be creative and choose an idea that stands out from the pack as original and innovative 
	\end{enumerate}
\item Think carefully before you choose a controversial topic: \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item  Academics are a sensitive lot, and in every field there are certain topics and positions that will send highly educated people into intellectual temper tantrums
	\item This doesn't mean you should avoid topics that push people's buttons
	\item However, if you choose a controversial topic, think carefully about whether it might restrict your employment, tenure, or publishing opportunities
	\end{enumerate}
\item Pick a topic that you already have some expertise about: \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item This will help preserve your sanity and get you out the door faster
	\item This isn't the time to explore a brand new area
	\item Along the way, take coursework and write class papers that will help you write your dissertation or thesis
	\end{enumerate}
\item Pick a manageable topic: \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item This is a huge project, but it isn't your life's research
	\item A good advisor will help you narrow down your topic so that you don't remain in graduate school for many long years
	\end{enumerate}
\item { \bf ``Whatever you do, hopefully in the end you will have found an area, a problem within that area, and a professor who works on that problem that are all a good (enough) match for you''}; see \url{http://mathgradblog.williams.edu/?p=10#more-10}
\item {\bf When I am considering a dissertation topic, and am investigating a particular technique/technology, ask the following questions:} \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item {\bf What is the value proposition of $[$this technique/technology that I'm considering$]$?}
	\item {\bf Can this technology be leveraged? If so, how?}
	\item {\bf How much can it improve design and verification productivity?}
	\item {\bf Have publications about this technique/technology gained a lot of traction in academia and in industry?}
	\item {\bf While these question should not be used to judge whether it is worth pursuing research in a particular topic, it can help me determine if there are opportunities to develop radical innovations since a considerable amount of research has not been done in this topic. That is, if this research topic is an emerging research topic, whatever ``little'' that I do can be considered significant.}
	\item {\bf I should pursue a research topic because I am passionate about it, and want to know more about it. As such, I want to carry out investigations and run experiments/simulations to determine aspects of it that no one else has, or prove that existing theories, models, or solutions are inadequate and develop better theories, models, or solutions.}
	\item {\bf However, I need not let this research pursuit remain purely an idealistic academic endeavor. Doing good research in my chosen research topic can lead to the creation of start-ups, opportunities of postdoc fellowships with world renowned research scientists and engineers, research positions in industry or nonprofit research institutes, or tenure-track positions in academia!!!}
	\item {\bf For example, with respect to the Ph.D. position at the Uni Trento, ask the following:} \vspace{-0.1cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item {\bf What is the value proposition of SMT-based formal verification?}
		\item {\bf Can this technology be leveraged? If so, how?}
		\item {\bf How much can it improve design and verification productivity?}
		\item {\bf Have research publications on SMT-based formal verification gained a lot of traction in academia and in industry?}
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{enumerate}
\item Factors for determining whether a research project is good (from \url{http://cacm.acm.org/blogs/blog-cacm/91102-choosing-successful-research/fulltext}): \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item {\bf Are you solving an important problem?} Why can't it be solved using today's technology? How will solving it make the world better?
	\item {\bf Is the market big enough?} In other words, who will benefit from you solving this problem, and by how much? Who will care?
	\item {\bf Is it simple?} The more complex an idea, the harder it is to communicate. If you can't explain it to an executive in one slide, they're unlikely champion your idea. If the product team doesn't understand your implementation, they will never take ownership of it.
	\item {\bf Is it a breakthrough?} What idea or technology do you have that your competitors don't? What prevents the product group from solving this problem on their own using known technology? Does your approach compare favorably to the alternatives, both in product and in the academic literature?
	\item Does this topic fascinate me?
	\item If you don't have a burning question to answers, don't pursue a PhD.
	\end{enumerate}
\item Finding a Research Topic: \url{http://compscigail.blogspot.com/2010/04/cra-w-grad-cohort-finding-research.html}
\end{enumerate}


%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\subsection{\hspace{0.1in} Advice for Initial Research on Chosen Topic}
\label{adviceinitialresearchchosentopic}

Advice for initial research on chosen topic: \vspace{-0.3cm}
\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -4pt
\item Planning phase: Spend about three months on the literature survey. I should continue to regularly read research publications on this topic for at least the first year, so that I can be ready to take my qualifying examination. For each research publication that I read, enter its {\sc Bib}\TeX\ database, summary, and paraphrase key points into my {\tt BibDesk} database.
\item Last viewed on June 6, 2010. See \url{http://www.dcsc.tudelft.nl/Education/Graduation%20Guide0910.pdf}.
\item Questions that I can ask concerning my approach to solve a research problem (or answer a research question): \vspace{-0.3cm}
	\begin{itemize} \itemsep -2pt
	\item What is GENI?
	\item Why is GENI doing this work?
	\item Tell us some background about Buckminster Fuller.
	\item How do power grids work?
	\item How technically feasible is the global grid? What percentage of it can be done now?
	\item What are the environmental implications; good and bad?
	\item What about the EMF issue? and the impact on GENI's work?
	\item How will this reduce acid rain, the greenhouse effect, and toxic wastes?
	\item How much will the project cost?
	\item Where will the money come from?
	\item How do the costs and benefits weigh out?
	\item Tell me more about the Computer Simulation Model.
	\item Who will administer this global system?
	\item How will this affect trade between countries?
	\item How can we expect countries to trust one another enough to become so interdependent?
	\item How will this effect developed and developing countries?
	\item How will it effect the economies of every nation?
	\item What is the criteria to determine the effect for each country?
	\item How will it make the leap and take advantage of remote renewable generation away from fossil fuels?
	\item Can the United States and the Soviet military and industrial complexes use this project as a means to convert from defense to civilian related interests?
	\item Who are the winners and the losers, if any?
	\item How do we set this up as a win/win solution?
	\item How will this reduce hunger?
	\item How will this reduce overpopulation?
	\item How do you propose this shift will occur? What's GENI's role in that?
	\item How is GENI funded?
	\item References: \vspace{-0.2cm}
		\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
		\item GENI, ``Medit Kit'', in {\it What's GENI?: Media Kits}, Global Energy Network Institute (GENI), San Diego, CA, July 28, 2009. Available online at: \url{http://www.geni.org/globalenergy/projects/news/media_kit/index.shtml}; last accessed on June 16, 2012.
		\item GENI, ``Frequently Asked Questions'', in {\it What's GENI?: Frequently Asked Questions}, Global Energy Network Institute (GENI), San Diego, CA, July 17, 2009. Available online at: \url{http://www.geni.org/globalenergy/faq/index.shtml}; last accessed on June 16, 2012.
		\end{enumerate}
	\end{itemize}
\end{enumerate}





%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\subsection{\hspace{0.1in} Advice for Comps/Prelims and Quals}
\label{adviceforcompsprelimsandquals}

Advice for Ph.D. comprehensive examination (or comprehensive exam/comps), Ph.D. preliminary examination (prelims), and Ph.D. qualifying exam (quals): \vspace{-0.3cm}
\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -4pt
\item it may consist of three written exams and an oral examination, only written examinations, or only oral examinations --- Check with the department
\item {\it \bf Are comps the same as the general examination (or generals)?}
\item comps/prelims are used to establish that the student is qualified to embark on Ph.D.-level research in the chosen field
\item quals are used to make sure that the student has a good plan for finishing the Ph.D., and that the committee (especially the advisor) and the student agree on the scope of the future research
\item study strategically and organize citations
\item keep up with class readings for seminar classes, which are classes that typically involve reading lots of papers and writing literature reviews (survey papers)
\item review my study notes for my classes, and formulate my ideas
\item read strategically: \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item while some departments disseminate a suggested reading list to graduate students, many require students to create their own
	\item students who write their own reading list, which typically includes journal articles and review papers, should make sure to organize it by topic and have a professor review it
	\item students can more readily synthesize and recall information if they read journal articles in a logical order, organized chronologically and by topic
	\item use a good reference management software, such as {\tt BibDesk}, to facilitate the organization of research publications that I have read 
	\end{enumerate}
\item keep abreast of my field: \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item professors often get ideas for questions from reading the latest research and controversies
	\item when studying for the exams, graduate students should take time to peruse the latest journals in their field
	\end{enumerate}
\item take detailed notes: \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item after reading an article, write down the main points of the article
	\item summarizing the research publication forces me to think critically as I read and the summaries provide material to study later
	\item { \bf Aim to read $>$100 papers, while writing my qualifying exam research proposal}
	\item make sure that I try to find the importance of every paper that I read
	\end{enumerate}
\item get organized: \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item spend time before quals using a good reference management software (e.g., {\tt BibDesk}) to get my research publications in order; { \bf I should continue to keep my research publications in order, as long as I am doing research}
	\item use {\tt BibDesk}, or other good reference management software (also, citation management software or citation software), to keep track of where I store digital copies of research publications on my computer ... By using keywords and/or the name of the author(s), I should be able to search for a research publication, and access its digital copy (if any) ... While I can tag each B{\scriptsize IB}\TeX\ entry in a B{\scriptsize IB}\TeX\ file, where I store all my references, I would still have to use Finder\texttrademark, and perhaps Spotlight\texttrademark, to find the digital copy (if any) of the research publication ... With a good reference management software, such as {\tt BibDesk}, I can use the keywords to access the B{\scriptsize IB}\TeX\ entry and the digital copy (if any) of the research publication
	\item use {\tt BibDesk}, or other good reference management software, to keep summaries and notes of research publications that I have read ... Again, I can enter such summaries and notes of research publications as comments after each B{\scriptsize IB}\TeX\ entry in a B{\scriptsize IB}\TeX\ file; however, I would have to take extra steps to locate the B{\scriptsize IB}\TeX\ entry and the digital copy (if any) of the research publication, and I may have trouble locating the B{\scriptsize IB}\TeX\ entry and the file ... With a good reference management software, such as {\tt BibDesk}, I can use the keywords to access the B{\scriptsize IB}\TeX\ entry, and my notes and summary for the research publication, as well as the digital copy (if any) of the research publication
	\item { \bf I should only use ONE reference management software for any significant period of time; else, I would lose some of the benefits of managing the references and their storage (digital copies of references)} --- I would otherwise need to keep porting my repository of research publications between reference management software
	\item create an index of research publications, which is organized by topic and author
	\item know where all my research publications are (don't scatter them around); use BibDesk to search for and keep track of research publications, and my corresponding notes and summaries for them
	\item make an itemized report of what information (my summaries and notes of publications) I have
	\end{enumerate}
\item seek advice from older students: \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item students who have already passed their comprehensive exams make great resources
	\item don't shy away from asking them (advanced Ph.D. students/candidates) for advice, or even for copies of their graded research proposals
	\item some colleges keep copies of past qualifying exams; { \bf if I need to take written and/or oral exam(s), try and see if I can get a copy of such past qualifying exams from the department's administrators for revision}
	\end{enumerate}
\item ask questions: \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item when you receive your exam questions, and if you are not sure what any question is asking, seek the faculty for clarification; else, I may end up taking a wrong guess about what the question is asking for, and give the wrong answer
	\item do not feel that I am admitting ignorance if I ask a question; the question can be expressed better
	\item it is better to risk looking uninformed than to give the wrong answer to a question (especially an answer that is off-topic)
	\end{enumerate}
\item read the directions: \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item under pressure, even normally meticulous students might fail to notice formatting details
	\item test-takers should note the number of pages (or marks, as a percentage) allotted to a question, and the formatting requirements of the comprehensive exam
	\item double check that I have answered every part of each (essay) question
	\end{enumerate}
\item be thorough, not encyclopedic: \vspace{-0.2cm}
	\begin{enumerate} \itemsep -2pt
	\item { \bf packing in too many citations is an all too common mistake}
	\item I can spend a lot of $[${ \bf unnecessary}$]$ time in the library or on the Internet, finding every article remotely relevant to the question ... { \bf Don't do that and waste my time; remember what Alicia Lowery told me about ``researching everything under the sun before I finish my thesis''}
	\item I need to be aware of the major trends, but I don't have to write an anthology
	\item while the intensive preparation can be exhausting, it pays
	\item I may not get any of those curveball questions; don't worry about such questions
	\item at the end of the $[$oral exam of the$]$ quals, elicit feedback from the professors $[$and research scientists$]$ on my thesis committee about my professional development; this can be validating in terms of the hard work that I have put in, and my research direction
	\end{enumerate}
\end{enumerate}
